The First Intellect's Intellection: Emanation, Not Reception
Commentary explains why the First Intellect's intellection is not due to multiple forms but to emanation, preserving the principle of the one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the First Intellect's knowledge cannot be through multiple forms in its essence, and how emanation preserves the principle 'from the one only one proceeds.'

Core Idea
The First Intellect knows by emanating, not by containing.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a misinterpretation regarding how the First Intellect knows. Some claimed it knows by having multiple forms inside it, but that would violate the fundamental philosophical principle that from the One only one can directly proceed. The commentator explains the correct view: the First Intellect's intellection is due to forms emanating from it into the Universal Soul, preserving both its simplicity and the emanation of multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'
This is a foundational rule in Islamic philosophy: the First Principle, being absolutely one, can directly produce only one thing. The First Intellect is that direct effect. Any multiplicity must come through intermediaries.
This principle is central to the argument in the passage.
2
Identify the mistaken interpretation
Some interpreters claimed that the First Intellect's intellection is due to multiple forms existing within its essence. The commentator strongly refutes this.
This is the view that leads to contradiction.
3
Recognize the logical impossibility
The passage states that a single thing cannot be both a receiver (to have forms inside it) and an agent (to produce them). If the First Intellect contained many forms, it would be composite and also an agent, which is impossible.
This argument relies on the impossibility of a single entity being both passive and active in the same respect.
4
Understand the correct solution: emanation
The First Intellect does not produce forms within itself; rather, it emanates them into the Universal Soul (the Preserved Tablet). Thus, its intellection consists in its being the source from which forms emanate.
This preserves both its simplicity and the principle of the one.
5
Connect to the phrase 'intellectuality due to emanation'
The commentator concludes that the First Intellect's intellectuality is 'for their emanation from him' — meaning its knowledge is not by containing forms but by causing them to flow to the next level of existence.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما نقلناه ظهر أن ما ذكره بعض الناظرين في حل هذه العبارة ناقلاً عن الشارح قدس سره مع عدم مساعدة العبارة له وعدم صحته في نفسه كما لا يخفى على الفطن إما افتراء على الشارح أو ناشئ من عدم تتبع الشارح لكلام الشيخ، وهو أنه يعني على أصل الفلاسفة لا يجوز أن يكون عقلية العقل الأول، لأجل صور كثيرة فيه، لأنه إذ ذاك يبطل قولهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد لأنه يلزم على هذا التقدير أن يصدر عن المبدأ أشياء كثيرة، إذ لا يجوز أن يوجدها العقل في نفسه، لأن الشيء الواحد لا يجوز أن يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً، بل لأنه يوجدها في النفس الكلية التي هي اللوح المحفوظ بلسان الشرع، وهذا معنى قوله وعقليته، لأجل فيضانها عنه.
And from what we have transmitted, it is clear that what some of the observers mentioned in explaining this expression, quoting from the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) – despite the expression not supporting him and it being incorrect in itself, as is not hidden from the intelligent – is either a slander against the commentator or arises from the commentator not having traced the shaykh's speech fully. And it is that he meant, according to the philosophers' principle, it is not permissible that the intellectuality of the First Intellect be due to many forms in it, because that would invalidate their statement 'the one does not proceed from him except the one', because on this supposition there would follow that many things proceed from the First Principle, since it is not permissible for the intellect to bring them into existence in itself, because a single thing cannot be both receiver and agent; rather, because it brings them into existence in the universal soul which is the Preserved Tablet in the language of the Law, and this is the meaning of his statement 'and his intellectuality is due to their emanation from him'.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents an argument concerning the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) and the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' (al-wāḥid lā yaṣdur ʿanhu illā wāḥid). The commentator criticizes an earlier interpreter's explanation of a statement. The correct understanding, according to the commentator, is that the First Intellect's intellection cannot be due to multiple forms existing in its essence, because that would imply multiplicity emanating from the First Principle, violating the principle. The First Intellect cannot both receive and produce forms since a single thing cannot be both passive and active. Instead, the intellect brings the forms into existence in the universal soul (al-nafs al-kulliyyah), which in religious language is the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ). Thus, the intellectuality of the First Intellect is not due to containing forms but due to being the source from which forms emanate ('fayḍān'). This preserves both the simplicity of the First and the emanation of multiplicity through intermediaries.

Key Terms
العقل الأول
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
فيضان
النفس الكلية
اللوح المحفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Intellect knows by containing multiple forms within its essence.
Correction: The passage argues that this would violate the principle of the one and is logically impossible; rather, the First Intellect knows by emanating forms to the Universal Soul.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why can the First Intellect not have multiple forms in itself?
A) Because the First Intellect is immaterial.
B) Because that would make it both receiver and agent, which is impossible for one thing.
C) Because the forms are only in the Preserved Tablet.
D) Because the principle of the one allows only one form.

Key Takeaway
The First Intellect's intellection is not through forms in its essence but through emanation to the Universal Soul, preserving the principle that from the one only one proceeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine Him 'receiving' information as we do? How does this lesson's emphasis on active emanation challenge your mental picture of God? Will you intentionally replace that image with one of pure, creative outpouring?
?
The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' forces a radical simplicity in the First Cause. Is there any area of your life where you attribute multiplicity or complexity to God's essence? What would it mean for your worship if you truly believed that God is absolutely simple and that all multiplicity comes through intermediaries?
?
A single thing cannot be both receiver and agent. In your own spiritual life, are you trying to both receive from God and act for Him simultaneously? How might this passage teach you to distinguish your role as a receptive servant from God's role as the sole agent? What practical step can you take today to surrender one area where you are acting as if you were the source?

Simple Actual Intellection and Its Divine Application
Commentary describes simple actual intellection and applies it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of simple actual intellection, how it differs from detailed actual intellection, and why God's knowledge is of this simple type yet identical to His essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is simple, not detailed, yet it is the source of all detail.

This passage distinguishes between types of intellection, focusing on a mode of knowledge that is complete yet undetailed. It uses a relatable example of a scholar retrieving an answer instantly. Then it applies this concept to the Necessary Existent, but with a crucial twist: in God, the simple intellect is identical to His essence, unlike the human soul. Understanding this helps clarify the nature of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key contrast between 'detailed' (tafṣīl) and 'simple' (basīṭ).
2
Identify the third type of intellection
The passage describes a type of knowledge that is 'actually present in complete actuality' but not detailed. This is the simple actual intellection. See how the example clarifies it.
This type is the principle of the second type (detailed actual).
3
Understand the relation between the second and third types
The passage states that the third type is the source (mabdaʾ) of the second type (detailed knowledge). This means detailed knowledge unfolds from this simple, holistic grasp.
Reflect on how this relates to the example.
4
Apply to the Necessary Existent
The passage says that the intellection of the Necessary Existent and separate intellects is of this simple kind. But note the critical difference: in God, the simple intellect is identical to His essence, not a separate attribute.
This guards against thinking God's knowledge is an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصلاً بالفعل التام لا على سبيل التفصيل بل على الوجه البسيط وهذا كمن كان عالماً بمسألة ثم سئل عنها فإنه يستحضر الجواب في ذهنه دفعة واحدة لكن لا على سبيل التفصيل فإن التفصيل إنما يحصل عند شروعه في بيان ذلك وهذا النوع الثالث مبدأ للنوع الثاني وتعقل الواجب والمفارقات من هذا القبيل إلا أن العقل البسيط للنفس مغاير لذاتها، وفي الواجب والمجردات عينها.
Actually present in complete actuality, not in a detailed way but in the simple mode. This is like someone who knows a question and then is asked about it: he brings the answer to mind all at once, but not in detail, for detail only occurs when he begins to explain it. And this third type is the principle of the second type. And the intellection of the Necessary Existent and the separate intellects is of this kind, except that the simple intellect of the soul is other than its essence, whereas in the Necessary Existent and the immaterial beings it is identical to the essence.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage classifies intellection (taʿaqqul) into three types, though it only describes two explicitly? Actually it describes the third type in detail and refers to the second. The context is from a work (al-Mulakhkhaṣ) describing the three types: potential, actual complete in detail, and actual complete in a simple manner. Here the commentator focuses on the third type: it is actual knowledge but not detailed; it is simple and holistic. An example is a scholar who knows an answer and can retrieve it instantly without the step-by-step detail; detail arises only when he begins to expound. This third type is the source (mabdaʾ) of the second type (detailed knowledge). The commentator then applies this to the Necessary Existent (Wājib al-Wujūd) and the separate intellects (mufāriqāt): their intellection is of this simple, immediate kind. However, a crucial difference is noted: in the human soul, the simple intellect (al-ʿaql al-basīṭ al-ḥāṣil) is other than the soul itself, but in the Necessary Existent and the immaterial beings, it is identical to the essence. Thus, God's knowledge is His essence, not an accident added to it.

Key Terms
تعقل
بالفعل التام
التفصيل
الوجه البسيط
العقل البسيط
المفارقات
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge of particulars requires division or detail in His essence, or that His knowledge is an accident added to His essence.
Correction: The passage shows that God's intellection is simple and undetailed, yet it is the source of all detail. Moreover, in God, the simple intellect is identical to His essence, so it is not an accidental attribute.

Checkpoint
How does the intellection of the Necessary Existent differ from that of the human soul according to this passage?
A) God's intellection is detailed, while human intellection is simple.
B) In God, the simple intellect is identical to His essence; in humans, it is other than the soul.
C) God's intellection is potential, while humans are actual.
D) Both are identical in mode but differ in content.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is a simple, undetailed act identical to His essence, not an accidental attribute, and it is the source of all detailed knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on detailed reasoning or on an immediate, simple recognition of God's presence? How can you cultivate a simple, undivided awareness of God in your daily life, rather than a fragmented, step-by-step faith?
?
If God's knowledge is simple and identical to His essence, what does that mean for how you approach seeking knowledge? Are you content with a superficial, detailed accumulation, or are you striving for a simple, essential understanding that transforms your soul?
?
What would it take for you to experience a simple, immediate awareness of a theological truth, like the scholar who instantly recalls the answer? What barriers—pride, doubt, distraction—prevent you from seeing God's truths with such clarity, and what will you do this week to remove one of them?

Resolving the Apparent Contradiction in Ibn Sina on Divine Knowledge
Commentary identifies a contradiction and resolves it via essence-concomitant distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent contradiction between Ibn Sina's statements in al-Shifa' and al-Isharat is resolved by distinguishing God's essence from its necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in God's knowledge is in the concomitants, not the essence itself.

Ibn Sina is known for two key principles: the First Intellect's intellection is not due to multiple forms, and 'from the one only one proceeds.' Yet in al-Isharat, he seems to contradict these by describing God's knowledge as acquired (ḥuṣūlī) with multiple intelligible forms. This lesson examines the commentator's resolution, which preserves God's simplicity by locating multiplicity in the concomitants of the essence, not the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow: first, the contradiction is stated, then the resolution is offered.
The passage is incomplete in the Arabic, but the core argument is present.
2
Identify the two positions of Ibn Sina
Look for the two principles Ibn Sina holds in al-Shifa' and then the contrasting view in al-Isharat. See how the commentator frames the contradiction.
The principles are: God's knowledge is not due to multiple forms (later clarified that multiplicity is in concomitants) and 'from one, only one proceeds.'
3
Understand the resolution
The key is the distinction between the essence and its concomitants (lawāzim). Multiplicity is attributed to the concomitants and their effects, which are posterior to the essence and not constitutive.
This preserves God's transcendence over multiplicity while accounting for the multiplicity of intelligibles.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this resolution implies: that the one God can be both a receiver (of forms as concomitants) and an agent (of effects). This harmonizes the two texts.
This is a sophisticated metaphysical move that avoids literal contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث وهو أن أبا علي مع أنه قائل بالأصلين المذكورين وقع منه في الإشارات ما يناقضه، حيث ذهب فيها إلى أن علم الله تعالى حصولي، وأن الصور العقلية متباينة متقررة في ذاته تعالى والكثرة الحاصلة في عقله للأشياء كثيرة في لوازم ذاته ومعلولاتها، وهي مترتبة على الذات متأخرة عن حقيقتها لا مقومة لها، فلا ينافي تنزه ذاته عن الكثرة، الذي دل عليه البرهان فهذا الكلام منه يدل على أن الواحد يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً، وأن الواحد يصدر عنه غير
And here there is a discussion: that Abū ʿAlī [Ibn Sīnā], despite being an adherent of the two aforementioned principles, there occurs from him in al-Ishārāt what contradicts them, for he held in it that God's knowledge is acquired (ḥuṣūlī) and that the intelligible forms are distinct and established in His essence – and the multiplicity occurring in His intellect of things is a multiplicity in the concomitants of His essence and their effects, and they are ordered upon the essence, posterior to its reality, not constitutive of it, so it does not contradict the transcendence of His essence from multiplicity, which the proof has indicated. So this statement of his indicates that the one can be receiver and agent, and that from the one proceeds other than [it...]
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage raises an apparent contradiction in Ibn Sīnā's (Abū ʿAlī) thought. In his al-Shifāʾ, he holds the two principles: the First Intellect's intellection is not due to multiple forms and the principle of 'from the one only one proceeds'. However, in al-Ishārāt, Ibn Sīnā seems to contradict these by stating that God's knowledge is acquired (ḥuṣūlī) and that multiple intelligible forms subsist in His essence. The commentator resolves this by explaining that the multiplicity is not in God's essence itself but in the concomitants (lawāzim) of His essence and their effects. These are ordered upon the essence but are posterior to it, not constitutive (muqawwima) of it. Thus, the essence remains transcendent over multiplicity. This resolution implies that the one entity (God) can be both a receiver (of forms as concomitants) and an agent (of the effects). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main point is clear: the apparent contradiction is resolved by distinguishing the essence from its necessary concomitants.

Key Terms
أبو علي
الإشارات
الشفاء
علم حصولي
الصور العقلية
لوازم الذات
التنزه عن الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina's texts are contradictory and that he changed his view.
Correction: The commentator shows that the contradiction is only apparent; Ibn Sina consistently holds that God's essence is simple, and the multiplicity is in the concomitants of the essence, which are not part of the essence itself.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the apparent contradiction between Ibn Sina's al-Shifa' and al-Isharat?
A) By denying the authenticity of al-Isharat.
B) By stating that the multiplicity in God's knowledge is in the concomitants of the essence, not the essence itself.
C) By claiming that Ibn Sina changed his view.
D) By asserting that God's knowledge is not intelligible.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's apparent contradiction is resolved by distinguishing God's essence from its concomitants: multiplicity pertains only to the latter, preserving divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself settling for a superficial understanding of God's attributes, avoiding the intellectual struggle to reconcile apparent contradictions? How does Ibn Sina's careful distinction challenge you to take your theology more seriously?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you imagine it as similar to your own, with multiple thoughts and ideas? How does this lesson's insistence that multiplicity is only in the concomitants, not the essence, force you to purify your mental image of God?
?
If God's essence is truly simple with no multiplicity even in knowledge, what does that mean for how you approach Him in worship? How does knowing that He knows you perfectly from His simple essence change your sense of being known?

Refutation of a Misinterpretation about the First Intellect
Refutes a misinterpretation about the First Intellect and many forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the First Intellect can be the source of many forms without violating the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'.

Core Idea
The First Intellect emanates forms, not contains them.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if the First Intellect contains many forms, wouldn't that violate the principle that from the One only one proceeds? The author refutes a misinterpretation of that very principle, clarifying how emanation into the Universal Soul resolves the apparent conflict. We'll examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the misinterpretation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Look for the false claim: that the First Intellect cannot have many forms because that would contradict 'from the One only one proceeds'.
The misinterpretation is attributed to 'some investigators' who quote the commentator.
2
Understand the refutation's key distinction
The refutation argues that the forms do not exist in the First Intellect itself; rather, they are emanated from it. Notice the distinction between being receptive (قابل) and active (فاعل). The One cannot be both, so it cannot receive forms, but it can emanate them.
This distinction is crucial to preserve the principle.
3
Recognize the role of the Universal Soul
The forms are placed in the Universal Soul (النفس الكلية), which is identified with the Preserved Tablet. This intermediary allows the First Intellect to be the source of many forms while only one thing proceeds directly from it.
This aligns with the philosophical system and scriptural language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما نقلناه ظهر أن ما ذكره بعض الناظرين في حل هذه العبارة ناقلاً عن الشارح قدس سره مع عدم مساعدة العبارة له وعدم صحته في نفسه كما لا يخفى على الفطن إما افتراء على الشارح أو ناشئ من عدم تتبع الشارح لكلام الشيخ، وهو أنه يعني على أصل الفلاسفة لا يجوز أن يكون عقلية العقل الأول، لأجل صور كثيرة فيه، لأنه إذ ذاك يبطل قولهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد لأنه يلزم على هذا التقدير أن يصدر عن المبدأ أشياء كثيرة، إذ لا يجوز أن يوجدها العقل في نفسه، لأن الشيء الواحد لا يجوز أن يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً، بل لأنه يوجدها في النفس الكلية التي هي اللوح المحفوظ بلسان الشرع، وهذا معنى قوله وعقليته، لأجل فيضانها عنه.
And by what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that what some of the investigators mentioned in solving this expression, quoting from the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) – while the expression does not support him and it is not correct in itself, as is not hidden from the astute – is either a fabrication against the commentator or arises from the commentator not following the Sheikh's speech; namely, that according to the philosophers' principle, it is not permissible for the First Intellect to have intellectuality due to many forms in it, because then their statement 'from the One only one proceeds' would be invalidated, since on this supposition many things would proceed from the Principle, because it is not permissible for it to place them in itself, for one thing cannot be both receptive and active, but rather because it places them in the Universal Soul, which is the Preserved Tablet in the language of the Law; and this is the meaning of his saying 'and its intellectuality is due to their emanation from it'.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage refutes a misinterpretation. A certain investigator, quoting the commentator, claimed that according to the philosophers the First Intellect cannot contain many forms because that would violate the principle that from the One only one proceeds. The refutation argues that this interpretation is false: the forms do not exist in the First Intellect itself, but emanate from it into the Universal Soul (the Preserved Tablet). The One cannot be both receptive and active; thus the forms are not received but emanated. The phrase 'its intellectuality is due to their emanation' means that the First Intellect's intellection consists in emanating forms, not containing them.

Key Terms
عقل الأول
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
قابل
فاعل
النفس الكلية
اللوح المحفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Intellect cannot contain many forms because that would violate the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'.
Correction: The forms are not contained in the First Intellect but emanated from it into the Universal Soul; the principle applies to what proceeds directly, and the First Intellect is only active (not receptive), so it can be the source of many forms through emanation.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, where do the many forms of the First Intellect reside?
A) In the First Intellect itself
B) In the Universal Soul (Preserved Tablet)
C) In the intellects of angels
D) They do not exist at all

Key Takeaway
The First Intellect's intellection consists in emanating forms into the Universal Soul, not in containing them, thereby preserving the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems to conflict with your understanding, do you rush to refute it based on your assumptions, or do you humble yourself to seek the correct interpretation as the author did here? How can you ensure that your intellect does not become a barrier to truth?
?
The principle 'from the One only one proceeds' is easily misunderstood. In your own life, are there areas where you cling to a principle without understanding its proper application, perhaps causing you to reject valid possibilities? What would it take for you to admit you might be wrong?
?
The First Intellect's activity is pure emanation without passivity. Reflect on your own actions: are you more often receptive or active in your relationship with God? How can you shift from a passive recipient of knowledge to an active emanator of good into the world?

Ibn Sina's Contradiction on Divine Knowledge
Ibn Sina's contradiction regarding divine knowledge and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina's statements in al-Isharat contradict his own principles that from the One only one proceeds and that the One cannot be receptive and active.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina undermines his own principle that from the One only one proceeds.

This lesson examines a key inconsistency in Ibn Sina's thought. Although he upholds that from the One only one can proceed and that the One cannot be both receptive and active, his discussion of divine knowledge in al-Isharat implies otherwise. We will see how his claim that multiple intellectual forms exist in God's essence contradicts his own foundational principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's two principles
Read the opening to recall the two principles Ibn Sina is said to hold: from the One only one proceeds, and the One cannot be receptive and active.
2
Analyze his position in al-Isharat
Notice that he describes God's knowledge as acquired (husuli) and states that multiple intellectual forms are established in His essence, implying multiplicity.
This directly contradicts the principle of oneness.
3
Understand the implication
The passage concludes that this makes the One both receptive and active, which is unacceptable according to his own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث وهو أن أبا علي مع أنه قائل بالأصلين المذكورين وقع منه في الإشارات ما يناقضه، حيث ذهب فيها إلى أن علم الله تعالى حصولي، وأن الصور العقلية متباينة متقررة في ذاته تعالى والكثرة الحاصلة في عقله للأشياء كثيرة في لوازم ذاته ومعلولاتها، وهي مترتبة على الذات متأخرة عن حقيقتها لا مقومة لها، فلا ينافي تنزه ذاته عن الكثرة، الذي دل عليه البرهان فهذا الكلام منه يدل على أن الواحد يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً، وأن الواحد يصدر عنه غير سائر الأحوال النفسانية، وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية العلم، وما يتوقف عليه البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياً، فتصور العلم بديهي ثم إنَّ هذه الحالة الوجدانية المسماة بالعلم ليست عدمية، لأنها ممتازة عن غيرها بالضرورة والعدم لا يكون كذلك، وأيضاً لو كانت عدماً لكانت عدم ما يقابلها وهو إما الجهل البسيط الذي هو عدم . قوله: ( وذلك يتوقف إلخ) فيه أن اللازم منه تصور العلم الجزئي بوجه ما بالضرورة ولا يلزم منه تصور العلم المطلق فضلاً عن أن يكون بديهياً والجواب أن المقصود تصوره بوجه ما وذلك لازم من تصور العلم الجزئي . معدومة . قوله: (ليست عدمية) أي عدم شيء، بل أمر محصل في نفسه سواء كانت موجودة أو قوله: ( والعدم لا يكون كذلك فيه أنَّ المعدومات، وكذا الأعدام متمايزة في نفسها، وإن لم تكن متمايزة في الخارج. قوله: (عدم ما يقابلها ) إذ لا يجوز أن يكون عدماً مطلقاً، وهو ظاهر ولا عدم شيء سوى ما يقابلها لاجتماعها معه . الواحد ثم إن كلامه هاهنا أيضاً مخالف لما أطبق عليه الفلاسفة من أن للعقل الأول جهات ثلاثة فصدر عنه ثلاثة أشياء : العقل الثاني، ونفس الفلك التاسع وجرمه كما سيأتي وإن كان إسناد نفس العقل التاسع مع ما فيه من الصور حينئذ إلى العقل الأول باعتبار وجوبه بالغير، كإسنادهم ما فيه من الكواكب المتكثرة إلى جهة واحدة في العقل الثامن والاعتراض الفلك الثامن مع الاعتراض . قوله : ( فتصور العلم بديهي قد سبق الاعتراض عليه بأنه لا يفيد بديهة الكنه . قوله : ( لأنها ممتازة عن غيرها أي نظراً إلى ذاتها وبحسب نفس الأمر بخلاف العدمات فإنها ممتازة بالإضافة . قوله : ( لكانت عدم ما يقابلها فيه دلالة على أن المراد بالعدمي هاهنا ما يكون العدم جزءاً من مفهومه لا المعدوم، وإلا فقد سبق في بحث التعين أنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء عدمياً بذلك المعنى كونه عدماً لشيء. قوله : ( وهو إما الجهل البسيط الذي هو عدم قيل : الجهل البسيط إنما يكون عدماً إذا كان مقابله الذي هو العلم موجوداً، فيتوقف مقدمة الدليل على المدعى وهو المصادرة.
And here is a discussion: that Abu Ali (Ibn Sina), despite being a proponent of the two mentioned principles, has something from him in al-Isharat that contradicts him, where he held that God's knowledge is acquired (husuli), and that the intellectual forms are distinct and established in His essence, and the multiplicity occurring in His intellect of things is multiplicity in the concomitants of His essence and their effects, and they are ordered after the essence, posterior to its reality, not constitutive of it; so it does not contradict the transcendence of His essence from multiplicity, which the proof has shown. So this speech of his indicates that the One is both receptive and active, and that the One produces other than all psychic states. And that depends on conceiving the essence of knowledge, and what the intuitive depends on is more proper to be intuitive, so the conception of knowledge is intuitive. Then this subjective state called knowledge is not a privation, because it is distinguished from others necessarily, and privation is not so. Also, if it were a privation, it would be a privation of its opposite, which is either simple ignorance, which is a privation. His saying: (And that depends, etc.) – in it [there is] that what is necessary from it is the conception of particular knowledge in some way necessarily, and it does not necessitate the conception of absolute knowledge, let alone it being intuitive. The answer: the intended is its conception in some way, and that is necessitated from the conception of particular knowledge. [Something] non-existent. His saying: (it is not a privation) – i.e., it is not the absence of something, but rather something realized in itself, whether it exists or... His saying: (and privation is not so) – in it [there is] that non-existent things, and likewise privations, are distinguished in themselves, even if they are not distinguished externally. His saying: (privation of its opposite) – because it is not permissible for it to be absolute privation, which is obvious, nor the privation of something other than its opposite, due to its coexistence with it. The one. Then his speech here is also contrary to what the philosophers have agreed upon, that for the First Intellect there are three aspects, and from it proceed three things: the Second Intellect, the soul of the ninth sphere, and its body, as will come; although attributing the soul of the ninth sphere with what it contains of forms then to the First Intellect in consideration of its necessity through another, like their attribution of what it contains of multiple planets to one aspect in the eighth intellect and the objection... His saying: (So the conception of knowledge is intuitive) – the objection to it has already preceded: that it does not give intuitiveness of the quiddity. His saying: (because it is distinguished from others) – i.e., with respect to its essence and according to the matter itself, contrary to privations, for they are distinguished by relation. His saying: (it would be a privation of its opposite) – in it there is an indication that what is meant by 'privative' here is that privation is part of its concept, not the privated thing; otherwise, it has preceded in the discussion of determination that it does not necessarily follow from a thing being privative in that sense that it is a privation of something. His saying: (which is either simple ignorance, which is a privation) – it is said: simple ignorance is only a privation when its opposite, which is knowledge, exists; so the premise of the proof depends on what is claimed, which is circularity.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (bahth) that raises two issues. First, it points out Ibn Sina's contradiction: although he holds the principle that from the One only one proceeds and that the One cannot be both receptive and active, in al-Isharat he claims divine knowledge is acquired and that multiple forms exist in God's essence as posterior concomitants. This implies the One can be both receptive and active. Second, it argues that knowledge is not a privation: it is an immediate, intuitive state (wijdaniyya) necessarily distinguished from other states, whereas privations are not. The argument is defended against objections: an objection that only particular knowledge is intuitive, not absolute, is answered by saying the intended conception is in some way, which follows from particular knowledge. The response also clarifies that privations are distinguished by relation, not by essence, and that 'privative' here means having privation as part of the concept. The final objection that simple ignorance is privation only conditionally (if its opposite exists) leads to circularity, so it fails.

Key Terms
علم حصولي
صور عقلية
كثرة
أحوال نفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's view on divine knowledge is consistent with his principles of emanation.
Correction: This passage shows that his claim of multiple intellectual forms in God's essence implies the One is receptive and active, contradicting his own principles.

Checkpoint
What do Ibn Sina's statements in al-Isharat imply according to the passage?
A) That the One is only active, not receptive
B) That the One is both receptive and active
C) That multiplicity in God's essence is impossible
D) That God's knowledge is innate (huduri)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's position on divine knowledge in al-Isharat contradicts his own principles that from the One only one proceeds and that the One cannot be receptive and active.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter contradictions in theologies like Ibn Sina's, do you feel a pull to force a reconciliation even when it is inconsistent? How does that tendency affect your own understanding of God's transcendence? Will you commit to accepting logical consistency even when it challenges comfortable explanations?
?
If the One truly cannot be receptive, what does that demand of your own intellectual approach to God? Are you willing to admit that your concepts and categories may fall short, or do you, like Ibn Sina, try to fit God into a framework that ultimately contradicts itself?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Knowledge as an Intuitive State, Not a Privation
Proof that knowledge is intuitive and not a privation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main argument that knowledge is not a privation because it is an intuitive state distinguished necessarily from other states.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a positive, intuitive state, not an absence or privation.

In this lesson, we move from the critique of Ibn Sina to a constructive argument about the nature of knowledge. The author asserts that knowledge is an intuitive (badihi) concept and that the subjective state called knowledge is not a privation. This is proven by its necessary distinctiveness from other states, unlike privations which are only distinguished by relation.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the claim that knowledge is intuitive
Read the opening sentence carefully: it depends on conceiving the essence of knowledge, and what the intuitive depends on is itself intuitive.
This establishes the epistemological foundation.
2
Understand the proof that knowledge is not privation
The passage gives two reasons: (1) knowledge is distinguished from other states necessarily, whereas privations are not distinguished in themselves; (2) if knowledge were a privation, it would be a privation of its opposite, but that leads to problems.
3
Identify the two possibilities for the opposite
The opposite of knowledge could be simple ignorance, which itself is a privation. This sets up the next lesson's objection.

Why does the passage argue that knowledge is not a privation?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to know that God exists, is that knowledge in your heart a positive, intuitive conviction that feels distinct from all other states, or is it merely the absence of doubt? This lesson proves that genuine knowledge must be the former. Be honest: which do you actually experience? If it is merely absence, what step will you take today to transform it into positive knowledge?
?
Consider a certainty you hold about Islamic creed. Can you pinpoint the moment when that certainty became intuitive—when the truth presented itself so clearly that you no longer needed to compare it to its opposite? If you cannot, does that mean your 'knowledge' might actually be a privation of ignorance? How will you seek that intuitive state?
?
The lesson says the conception of knowledge itself is intuitive. Have you ever stopped to reflect on what it means to 'know' something? When you recite 'I know that Allah is one,' do you feel the intuitive reality of that statement, or is it just words? What would change in your worship if you truly experienced knowledge as a positive state?

Defending the Proof: Objections and Clarifications
Objections and answers to the proof that knowledge is not privation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the proof that knowledge is not a privation and how the author defends his position, including the circularity argument.

Core Idea
Objections claim knowledge is not fully intuitive and that privations can be distinct, but the author clarifies key distinctions.

This lesson tackles the objections brought against the previous argument that knowledge is not a privation. Critics argue that only particular knowledge is intuitive, not absolute knowledge, and that privations can indeed be distinguished. The author responds by clarifying that the conception of knowledge in some way is sufficient, and that privations are distinguished by relation, not essence. He also points out a circularity in the objection about simple ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Follow the first objection and answer
The objection says only particular knowledge is intuitive, not absolute knowledge. The answer: the intended conception is in some way, which follows from particular knowledge.
This defends the claim that knowledge is intuitive.
2
Understand the second objection about privation
It objects that privations are also distinguished in themselves. The author contradicts this, saying privations are distinguished by relation, not by essence.
3
Examine the circularity argument
The last objection claims simple ignorance is privation only when its opposite (knowledge) exists. This makes the premise circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And here is a discussion: that Abu Ali (Ibn Sina), despite being a proponent of the two mentioned principles, has something from him in al-Isharat that contradicts him, where he held that God's knowledge is acquired (husuli), and that the intellectual forms are distinct and established in His essence, and the multiplicity occurring in His intellect of things is multiplicity in the concomitants of His essence and their effects, and they are ordered after the essence, posterior to its reality, not constitutive of it; so it does not contradict the transcendence of His essence from multiplicity, which the proof has shown. So this speech of his indicates that the One is both receptive and active, and that the One produces other than all psychic states. And that depends on conceiving the essence of knowledge, and what the intuitive depends on is more proper to be intuitive, so the conception of knowledge is intuitive. Then this subjective state called knowledge is not a privation, because it is distinguished from others necessarily, and privation is not so. Also, if it were a privation, it would be a privation of its opposite, which is either simple ignorance, which is a privation. His saying: (And that depends, etc.) – in it [there is] that what is necessary from it is the conception of particular knowledge in some way necessarily, and it does not necessitate the conception of absolute knowledge, let alone it being intuitive. The answer: the intended is its conception in some way, and that is necessitated from the conception of particular knowledge. [Something] non-existent. His saying: (it is not a privation) – i.e., it is not the absence of something, but rather something realized in itself, whether it exists or... His saying: (and privation is not so) – in it [there is] that non-existent things, and likewise privations, are distinguished in themselves, even if they are not distinguished externally. His saying: (privation of its opposite) – because it is not permissible for it to be absolute privation, which is obvious, nor the privation of something other than its opposite, due to its coexistence with it. The one. Then his speech here is also contrary to what the philosophers have agreed upon, that for the First Intellect there are three aspects, and from it proceed three things: the Second Intellect, the soul of the ninth sphere, and its body, as will come; although attributing the soul of the ninth sphere with what it contains of forms then to the First Intellect in consideration of its necessity through another, like their attribution of what it contains of multiple planets to one aspect in the eighth intellect and the objection... His saying: (So the conception of knowledge is intuitive) – the objection to it has already preceded: that it does not give intuitiveness of the quiddity. His saying: (because it is distinguished from others) – i.e., with respect to its essence and according to the matter itself, contrary to privations, for they are distinguished by relation. His saying: (it would be a privation of its opposite) – in it there is an indication that what is meant by 'privative' here is that privation is part of its concept, not the privated thing; otherwise, it has preceded in the discussion of determination that it does not necessarily follow from a thing being privative in that sense that it is a privation of something. His saying: (which is either simple ignorance, which is a privation) – it is said: simple ignorance is only a privation when its opposite, which is knowledge, exists; so the premise of the proof depends on what is claimed, which is circularity.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (bahth) that raises two issues. First, it points out Ibn Sina's contradiction: although he holds the principle that from the One only one proceeds and that the One cannot be both receptive and active, in al-Isharat he claims divine knowledge is acquired and that multiple forms exist in God's essence as posterior concomitants. This implies the One can be both receptive and active. Second, it argues that knowledge is not a privation: it is an immediate, intuitive state (wijdaniyya) necessarily distinguished from other states, whereas privations are not. The argument is defended against objections: an objection that only particular knowledge is intuitive, not absolute, is answered by saying the intended conception is in some way, which follows from particular knowledge. The response also clarifies that privations are distinguished by relation, not by essence, and that 'privative' here means having privation as part of the concept. The final objection that simple ignorance is privation only conditionally (if its opposite exists) leads to circularity, so it fails.

Key Terms
علم جزئي
علم مطلق
بديهة الكنه
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof that knowledge is not a privation is flawed because only particular knowledge is intuitive.
Correction: The author responds that the conception of knowledge in some way is intuitive, which is enough.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that only particular knowledge, not absolute knowledge, is intuitive?
A) Absolute knowledge is also intuitive by itself
B) The intended conception is in some way, which follows from particular knowledge
C) Particular knowledge is not intuitive
D) The objection is invalid because knowledge is not intuitive

Key Takeaway
Objections to the proof that knowledge is not a privation are answered by clarifying that knowledge is intuitive in some way, privations are distinguished by relation, and the objection about simple ignorance leads to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something is 'self-evident' in your faith, only to realize you never truly examined its foundations? Where in your life might you be relying on 'intuitive knowledge' without scrutinizing it, as the objection warned against? This week, pick one such belief and test it against the Qur'an and Sunnah seriously.
?
The author shows that an objection against the proof is circular: it assumes what it tries to prove. Are there arguments you accept about religious matters that are actually circular – where you believe something simply because you already believe it? Ask Allah to show you any such blind spots in your reasoning.
?
Simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) is only blameworthy when the opposite knowledge is possible and expected. What knowledge about Allah, His names, or His attributes do you have the means to acquire but have neglected out of laziness or procrastination? Make a concrete plan to remove that ignorance today.

The Substitution Argument and Its Refutation
Presents the substitution argument and its refutation using divine knowledge and other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the substitution argument against a single knowledge relating to multiple objects and its refutation through the principle that multiple relations do not require multiple essences.

Core Idea
Multiple relations do not require multiple essences.

The author presents a second argument, the substitution argument, which claims that a single knowledge cannot relate to multiple objects because knowledge of one would substitute for knowledge of another. This argument is then refuted by pointing to God's single knowledge relating to many things and other unitary qualities like blackness with multiple relations. This lesson unpacks the logic and the crucial insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the claim that if a single knowledge related to two knowns, then one would take the place of the other, and why that is considered false.
Notice the logical structure: premise → consequence → falsehood.
2
Examine the refutation
See how the refutation uses God's knowledge and a single blackness to show that multiple relations can coexist in a single essence without requiring multiplicity.
The blackness example lists three distinct relations.
3
Reflect on the principle
Understand the key takeaway: multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence. The essence with one relation does not substitute for the essence with another relation.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the purpose of the blackness example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فلا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر، هذا دليل ثان على المذهب الثاني وهو أن يقال : لو تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين لسد العلم بأحدهما مسد العلم بالآخر ضرورة أن الشيء يسد مسد نفسه، والتالي باطل ) فإنَّ التعلق بالمعلوم (داخل) في حقيقته أي حقيقة العلم فإذا علم أحد المعلومين كان التعلق به داخلاً في هذا العلم، دون التعلق بالمعلوم الآخر، وإذا علم الآخر انعكس الحال، فلا يتصور قيام العلم بأحدهما مقام العلم بالآخر، (ونقض) هذا الدليل الثاني (بعلم الله تعالى، فإنه جار فيه مع كونه متعلقاً بأمور متعددة، (وبسائر) أي ونقض أيضاً بسائر الهويات المتعلقة بأشياء متعددة كالسواد الواحد، فإن له تعلقاً بالفاعل الموجد، وتعلقاً آخر بالمحل القابل، وتعلقاً ثالثاً بالزمان الذي وجد فيه، إلى غير ذلك . فتعدد التعلقات لا يقتضي تعددا في الذات وليس يلزم من وحدة الذات أن تكون هي مأخوذة مع تعلق مخصوص سادة مسدها مأخوذة مع تعلق آخر
And also, one does not take the place of the other. This is a second proof for the second position, which is to say: if a single knowledge related to two known things, then knowledge of one would take the place of knowledge of the other, necessarily because a thing takes the place of itself, and the consequent is false. For the relation to the known is (included) in its essence, i.e. the essence of knowledge. So if one of the known things is known, the relation to it is included in this knowledge, without the relation to the other known thing; and if the other is known, the situation is reversed. Thus it is inconceivable that knowledge of one takes the place of knowledge of the other. And this second proof is refuted by (the knowledge of God Most High), for it applies to it despite its being related to multiple things. (And by other) i.e., it is also refuted by other attributes related to multiple things, such as a single blackness, for it has a relation to the efficient cause, another relation to the receptive substrate, a third relation to the time in which it exists, and so on. Thus multiplicity of relations does not entail multiplicity in the essence, and it does not follow from the unity of the essence that it, taken with a specific relation, takes the place of itself taken with another relation.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against a single knowledge relating to multiple objects: if it did, knowledge of one object would substitute for knowledge of the other (since a thing substitutes for itself), which is false because the relation to one known is distinct from the relation to another. The refutation uses the example of God's single knowledge relating to many things, and also other attributes like a single blackness that has multiple relations (to cause, substrate, time). Thus multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence, nor does the essence with one relation replace the essence with another relation.

Key Terms
سد مسد
تعلق
علم الله
هويات
سواد واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a single quality has multiple relations, then the quality must be multiple.
Correction: The example of blackness shows that a single quality can have multiple relations (to cause, substrate, time) without becoming multiple.

Checkpoint
What does the example of a single blackness serve to refute?
A) That knowledge cannot relate to multiple objects.
B) That multiple relations require multiple essences.
C) That God's knowledge is not relevant.
D) That a thing cannot take the place of itself.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of relations does not entail multiplicity of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Objection of Dispensing with Multiple Attributes
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that one attribute could replace multiple attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that his position on divine attributes could lead to dispensing with multiple attributes by distinguishing between homogeneous and heterogeneous effects.

Core Idea
One attribute can cause two homogeneous effects but not heterogeneous ones.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses a potential objection to his earlier argument that a single knowledge can attach to two objects. The objector claims that if one knowledge can produce two attributes of knowing, then one attribute could produce both knowledge and power, eliminating the need for multiple attributes. Al-Jurjani responds by clarifying that a single attribute can only necessitate effects of the same kind, not different kinds, and that the objection only applies to a different theoretical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the objection
Notice how the passage starts by denying that the objection follows. Find the objector's implied claim that one attribute could dispense with multiple attributes.
The objection is: if one knowledge can necessitate two attributes of knowing, then one attribute could necessitate both knowledge and power.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between homogeneous effects (like two attributes of knowing) and heterogeneous effects (like knowledge and power). Pay attention to why this distinction matters.
The refutation relies on the absence of a common factor (al-jāmi`) between the two situations.
4
Note the additional point about 'al-hal'
The passage ends by noting that the consequence only follows for the proponent of al-hal, not for the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم من ذلك أي من جواز تعلق علم واحد بذينك المعلومين تارة، وتعلق علمين بهما أخرى الاستغناء عن تعدد الصفات بأن يقال: لو جاز أن يكون علم واحد موجباً للعالمية بالسواد والعالمية بالبياض مع الاتفاق على أنه إذا تعدد العلم بهما كان موجباً للعالميتين أيضاً، لكانت الصفة الواحدة موجبة لحكمين متغايرين كالصفات المتعددة، وحينئذ جاز أن تكون صفة واحدة موجبة للعالمية والقادرية معاً، فلا حاجة إلى إثبات صفات متعددة للأحكام المختلفة، وهو باطل بالضرورة والاتفاق. فإنه أي ما ذكرتموه من الاستدلال تمثيل أيضاً كما مر خال عن الجامع لجواز أن تكون صفة واحدة موجبة لحكمين متجانسين كالعالميتين ويمتنع إيجابها لحكمين متخالفين، كالعالمية والقادرية على أنه إنما يلزم القائل بالحال.
And it does not follow from that – i.e., from the permissibility of a single knowledge attaching to those two known things sometimes, and two knowledges attaching to them at other times – the dispensation from multiple attributes, as one might say: If it were permissible that a single knowledge necessitates the attribute of knowing black and the attribute of knowing white, while agreeing that when knowledge of them is multiple, it also necessitates two attributes, then one attribute would necessitate two different effects like multiple attributes, and in that case it would be permissible for one attribute to necessitate omniscience and omnipotence together, and there would be no need to establish multiple attributes for different rulings, but that is false necessarily and by consensus. For that – i.e., the reasoning you mentioned – is an example as well, as previously, lacking the common factor, since it is permissible for one attribute to necessitate two homogeneous effects like two attributes of knowing, but it is impossible for it to necessitate two heterogeneous effects like knowledge and power, except that it only follows for the proponent of the state (al-hal).
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of the position by addressing a potential objection: that allowing a single knowledge to produce two attributes of knowing (e.g., knowing black and knowing white) would lead to the conclusion that a single attribute could produce both knowledge and power, thus making multiple attributes unnecessary. Al-Jurjani refutes this by distinguishing between homogeneous and heterogeneous effects. He argues that a single attribute can indeed produce two homogeneous effects (like two instances of knowledge), but it cannot produce two different types of effects (like knowledge and power). The objector's example is invalid because it lacks the common factor required for analogy. Furthermore, this consequence only applies to the proponent of the theory of 'al-hal' (state), not to the Ash'ari position. Thus, the objection does not undermine the original claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a single knowledge can cause two effects of the same kind, it can also cause effects of different kinds, thus eliminating the need for multiple attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a single attribute can only necessitate homogeneous effects (e.g., two instances of knowing), not heterogeneous effects (e.g., knowledge and power), so multiple attributes remain necessary for different types of rulings.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does allowing a single knowledge to produce two instances of 'knowing' not lead to dispensing with multiple attributes?
A) Because the proponent of al-hal would object.
B) Because a single attribute cannot necessitate two homogeneous effects.
C) Because a single attribute can necessitate two homogeneous effects but not two heterogeneous ones.
D) Because the analogy lacks a common factor and only applies to al-hal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection by distinguishing between homogeneous and heterogeneous effects, showing that a single attribute cannot necessitate different types of effects, thus preserving the need for multiple attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simplicity of Intellect and Composite Knowledge
Critique of composite mental image and citation from Al-Shifa on simple knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the simplicity of the intellect is compatible with its knowledge of composite things, based on Avicenna's argument.

Core Idea
The simple intellect is a principle of detailed knowledge, not a composite.

This lesson tackles a classic puzzle: if the intellect is simple and without parts, how can it grasp composite realities? The author, building on Avicenna's Al-Shifa, argues that simplicity does not preclude the intellect from being a source of detailed knowledge. The passage introduces the concept of simple knowledge that emanates images, showing that the intellect's simplicity is a strength, not a limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the sharp criticism of the opposing view.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
The passage begins by rejecting the claim that the mental image is composite. Identify how this contradicts Al-Shifa and what alternative is proposed.
3
Examine the citation from Al-Shifa
The passage quotes Avicenna to describe simple knowledge as a single source that emanates images. Read that citation closely and ask: how does this solve the puzzle?
4
Reflect on the relationship between simple and detailed knowledge
Consider how simple knowledge (like understanding a whole) relates to detailed knowledge (like understanding parts). The passage suggests the former is productive of the latter.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مركبة من صور (متعددة هذا مخالف لما في الشفاء من أنه عقل بسيط لا تكثر فيه أصلا وأن تعقل الواجب، والمفارقات من هذا القبيل والفرق أنه عين الذات فيها وزائد على الذات فينا كما مر منقولاً عن المباحثات والظاهر أنه بسيط متعلق بالكل من حيث هو كل، ويجوز أن يكون العارض بسيطاً دون المعروض كالوحدة والتأليف والأطراف، نعم إن حصوله موقوف على حصول الأجزاء وصيرورتها مخزونة عند النفس كما يشهد به الوجدان . قوله : ( كان العلم بها على وجه أقوى الخ) في الشفاء الثاني : العلم البسيط الذي ليس من شأنه أن يكون له في نفسه صورة بعد صورة، لكن هو واحد يفيض منه الصور في قابل الصور فذلك علم فاعل للشيء الذي نسميه علماً فكرياً ومبدأ له، وذلك هو القوة العقلية من النفس المتشاكلة للعقول الفعالة وأما التفصيل فهو للنفس من حيث هو نفس فما لم يكن له ذلك لم يكن له علم نفساني، انتهى . ولا خفاء في أن كلامه يدل على أن العلم البسيط كالخلاف للعلم
His statement: 'Composed of multiple images' – this contradicts what is in Al-Shifa that [the intellect] is a simple intellect with no multiplicity at all, and that the intellection of the Necessary and the separate [intellects] is of this kind, and the difference is that He is identical with the essence in them and additional to the essence in us, as narrated from Al-Mubahathat. And the apparent [view] is that it is simple, relating to the whole insofar as it is a whole, and it is permissible that the accident be simple without the subject, like unity, composition, and extremities. Yes, its occurrence depends on the occurrence of the parts and their becoming stored in the soul, as is witnessed by intuition. His statement: 'Knowledge of them was stronger, etc.' – in Al-Shifa the second: 'The simple knowledge that is not such as to have in itself image after image, but it is one from which images emanate into the recipient of images, so that is productive knowledge of the thing that we call discursive knowledge and its principle. That is the rational power of the soul that conforms to the active intellects. As for detailed knowledge, it belongs to the soul insofar as it is a soul, and if it does not have that, it does not have psychic knowledge.' End. And it is not hidden that his words indicate that simple knowledge is like the opposite of knowledge.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a critical comment on the claim that the mental image is composed of multiple images. It argues that this contradicts Avicenna's view in Al-Shifa that the intellect is a simple intellect with no multiplicity, especially in the intellection of God and separate intellects. The difference between us and them is that in them the essence is identical with the intellection, while in us it is additional. The passage then suggests that the accident (the knowledge or image) can be simple even if the subject (the composite) is not, like unity or composition. It then cites a passage from Al-Shifa discussing simple knowledge: it is a single thing from which images emanate, and this is the source of discursive knowledge. Detailed knowledge pertains to the soul as soul. The passage concludes that this shows simple knowledge is different from ordinary knowledge.

Key Terms
العقل البسيط
العارض البسيط
الشفاء
التعقل
العقول الفعالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the intellect must be composite if it knows composite objects.
Correction: The passage shows that the intellect can be simple and still produce detailed knowledge through emanation, as the simple knowledge is a principle that gives rise to composite images without itself being composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage from Al-Shifa, what is the nature of simple knowledge?
A) It is composed of multiple images
B) It is a single thing that emanates images into the recipient
C) It is the same as detailed knowledge
D) It cannot be attributed to the soul

Key Takeaway
Simple knowledge is a unified source that produces detailed understanding without itself being composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is Total Knowledge Possible for God?
Examines opinions on attributing total knowledge to God and sets a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether total knowledge can be attributed to God and the condition that makes it impossible.

Core Idea
Total knowledge is valid for God only if it does not require ignorance of detail.

This passage introduces a key debate in Islamic theology about the nature of God's knowledge. Scholars differ on whether God possesses 'total knowledge' (al-ilm al-ijmālī) in addition to detailed knowledge. The correct position hinges on whether total knowledge implies ignorance of specifics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: it presents a debate and a conditional resolution.
The passage is incomplete; the response to the objection cuts off.
2
Identify the two positions
Note which scholars allow total knowledge for God (al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila) and which forbid it (many Ash'aris and Abu Hashim).
3
Understand the condition
The correct view makes a condition: if total knowledge requires ignorance of detail, it is impossible for God; otherwise it is not impossible.
This is the central point.
4
Consider the objection and response
The objector argues that denying total knowledge to God means denying Him a knowledge that creatures possess. The response begins but is incomplete.
Think about what the response might be: perhaps it is a different kind of knowledge.
5
Reflect on the implications
Why does this distinction matter? It preserves God's omniscience without imperfection.
Total knowledge without ignorance is essentially detailed knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرعان : الأول : العلم الإجمالي) على تقدير جواز ثبوته في نفسه ( هل يثبت لله تعالى أم لا؟ جوزه القاضي والمعتزلة، ومنعه كثير من أصحابنا وأبو هاشم والحق أنه إن اشترط فيه ) أي في العلم الإجمالي ( الجهل بالتفصيل امتنع عليه تعالى وإلا فلا يمتنع فإن قيل: فينتفي حينئذ عنه تعالى علم حاصل للمخلوق ) وهو العلم الإجمالي ( قلنا : نعم وهو) أي ذلك العلم المنتفي عنه تعالى
Two subsidiary issues: The first: Total knowledge – on the assumption that it is possible in itself, is it established for God, exalted, or not? Al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila permitted it, and many of our companions and Abu Hashim prevented it. The correct view is that if it [i.e., total knowledge] requires ignorance of detail, it is impossible for Him, exalted; otherwise it is not impossible. If it is said: Then there would be negated from Him, exalted, a knowledge that exists for the creature – that is, total knowledge – we say: Yes, and it [i.e., that knowledge negated from Him] is...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the attribution of total (ijmālī) knowledge to God. It presents two subsidiary issues (far'ān), starting with the first: Can total knowledge be attributed to God? It notes that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Mu'tazila allow it, while many Ash'aris and Abu Hashim deny it. The correct position (al-ḥaqq) is conditional: if total knowledge requires ignorance of detail, then it is impossible for God (who knows all things in detail); otherwise, it is not impossible. Then it anticipates an objection: if we deny total knowledge to God, then we are denying Him a type of knowledge that creatures possess. The response is 'yes, and it' – but the text cuts off with 'وهو' before completing the thought.

Key Terms
العلم الإجمالي
الله تعالى
الجاه بالتفصيل
القاضي
المعتزلة
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Total knowledge is inherently imperfect and cannot be attributed to God.
Correction: Total knowledge is only imperfect if it requires ignorance of detail; if it does not, it is not impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for total knowledge to be impossible for God according to the correct view?
A) If it is based on universal concepts
B) If it requires ignorance of detail
C) If it applies to composite objects
D) If it is simple

Key Takeaway
The attribution of total knowledge to God is permissible only if it does not entail ignorance of detail; otherwise it is impossible.

The passage shows that different scholars had different positions. Do you tend to accept beliefs uncritically because they come from a group you trust? How can you examine your own beliefs based on evidence and conditions, as this passage does?

The Possibility of Summary Knowledge for God
Debate on summary knowledge and its attribution to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether summary knowledge can be attributed to God and the crucial condition that determines its possibility.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is not necessarily impossible for God; it depends on whether it implies ignorance.

In this passage from Sanusi's commentary, we examine the first branch of a theological discussion: can summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali) be attributed to God? Different schools of thought have weighed in, and the author clarifies the key condition: if summary knowledge requires ignorance of details, it cannot apply to God; otherwise, it is possible. We'll follow the argument, objections, and final resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse every detail. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structured argument: positions, condition, objection, counter-objection, and further sub-arguments.
2
Identify the main positions
The passage lists who affirmed and who denied summary knowledge for God. Al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila affirmed; many Ash'aris and Abu Hashim denied. Note these groups.
This sets up the conflict that the author resolves.
3
Understand the condition and its implications
The author says the truth depends on a condition: if summary knowledge includes ignorance of details, it is impossible for God; otherwise not. Reflect on why this condition is crucial.
This is the key logical move that resolves the disagreement.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objection claims that denying summary knowledge to God would negate a type of knowledge that creatures have. The author responds by explaining that the negated knowledge is different from detailed knowledge and is actually stronger.
Consider how this strengthens the argument for possibility.
5
Examine the discussion of one knowledge
The passage addresses a second sub-argument about whether one mental form can correspond to many realities. The author shows that in summary knowledge, a composite form corresponds to a composite, which is unproblematic.
This refines the concept of summary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرعان : الأول : العلم الإجمالي) على تقدير جواز ثبوته في نفسه ( هل يثبت لله تعالى أم لا؟ جوزه القاضي والمعتزلة، ومنعه كثير من أصحابنا وأبو هاشم والحق أنَّه إِنْ اشترط فيه ) أي في العلم الإجمالي ( الجهل بالتفصيل امتنع عليه تعالى وإلا فلا يمتنع فإن قيل: فينتفي حينئذ عنه تعالى علم حاصل للمخلوق ) وهو العلم الإجمالي ( قلنا : نعم وهو) أي ذلك العلم المنتفي عنه تعالى غير التفصيلي وأنه علم للمفارقات الفعالة فهو أقوى فإنه علم بجميع الأجزاء دفعة واحدة من تقدم وتأخر يلزمان لأجل المادة وعوارضها . قوله: (ولا) علم بها في هذه الحالة (الخ قد يقال : إن المعلوم لنا في هذه الحالة هو مفهوم الشيء لكن من حيث اتحاده بتلك الأفراد إذ لا نعلم من تلك الأفراد إلا شيئاً فلم يتعلق العلم إلا بمعلوم واحد والفرق إنما هو باعتبار علمه من حيث هو وباعتبار العلم من حيث اتحاده بما صدق عليه . قوله : ( وأما قوله العلم الواحد (الخ لا شك أن هذا القول من الإمام مبني على أن العلم هو الصورة الحاصلة من المعلوم فإنه قائل بالوجود الذهني، ثم إن كان مراده أن العلم الواحد لا يكون علماً بمعلومات كثيرة مطلقاً كما هو الظاهر ،فجوابه ما ذكره الشارح وإن كان مراده إبطال ما لزم في صورة العلم الإجمالي بزعمه ويكون معنى كلامه أن العلم الواحد أي الصورة الواحدة لا تكون صورة مطابقة بمعلومات كثيرة مختلفات الحقائق بأن يكون تمام حقيقته كل منها لم يندفع بما ذكره الشارح بل جوابه حينئذ أن يقال : مطابقة صور واحدة لتمام حقيقة كل من المعلومات المتخالفة في الحقيقة بالمعنى الذي أشير إليه غير ما لزم في العلم الإجمالي، فإن اللازم فيه على ما صوره الشارح مطابقة صورة مركبة للمركب من حيث هو، ويتضمن مطابقة أجزاء هذه الصورة المركبة لأجزاء ذلك المركب على التوزيع ولا محذور فيه قطعاً . فالمنفي هو العلم المقرون بالجهل وهذا القيد يجب انتفاؤه عنه تعالى : ( وبالجملة عنه تعالى هو القيد أعني كونه مع الجهل وأنه لا يوجب نفي أصل العلم) بل هو ثابت له مجرداً عن ذلك القيد الذي يستحيل عليه تعالى.
Two branches: The first: Summary knowledge - on the assumption that its existence is possible in itself, is it affirmed for God Almighty or not? Al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila affirmed it, but many of our companions and Abu Hashim denied it. The truth is that if ignorance of detail is posited as a condition in it (i.e., in summary knowledge), then it is impossible for Him; otherwise it is not impossible. If it is said: Then there would be negated from Him a knowledge that exists for the creature (i.e., summary knowledge). We say: Yes, and it (i.e., that knowledge negated from Him) is other than detailed knowledge, and it is knowledge of the active separate intelligences, so it is stronger, for it is knowledge of all parts at once without temporal precedence or delay that are necessary due to matter and its accidents. His statement: 'And there is no knowledge of them in this state' etc. It may be said: That which is known to us in this state is the concept of the thing, but from the perspective of its union with those individuals, for we only know from those individuals something, so knowledge is only attached to one known object; the difference is only by considering the knowledge in itself and by considering the knowledge in terms of its union with what it applies to. His statement: 'As for his statement that one knowledge...' etc. There is no doubt that this statement of the Imam is based on the fact that knowledge is the form obtained from the known, for he is a proponent of mental existence. Then, if he intends absolutely that one knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things, as is apparent, its answer is what the commentator mentioned. But if he intends to refute what is entailed in the case of summary knowledge in his claim, and the meaning of his statement is that one knowledge - i.e., one form - cannot be a form that corresponds to many things differing in reality such that each one constitutes its entire reality, then it is not repelled by what the commentator mentioned. Rather, the answer then is to say: The correspondence of a single form to the entire reality of each of the differing pieces of information in reality, in the sense indicated, is other than what is entailed in summary knowledge. For what is entailed in it, according to what the commentator described, is the correspondence of a composite form to the composite as such, and it entails the distributional correspondence of the parts of this composite form to the parts of that composite, and there is absolutely no difficulty in that. So what is negated is knowledge coupled with ignorance, and this condition must be negated of Him. In summary, for Him is the condition, namely its being with ignorance, and that does not necessitate the negation of the essence of knowledge; rather, it is affirmed for Him stripped of that condition which is impossible for Him.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is the first branch of a discussion on summary knowledge (al-ilm al-ijmali). It asks whether summary knowledge can be attributed to God. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and the Mu'tazila affirmed it, while many Ash'aris and Abu Hashim denied it. The author states the truth: if summary knowledge is defined as including ignorance of details, it is impossible for God; otherwise it is not impossible. An objection is raised that if summary knowledge is negated from God, then a type of knowledge existing in creatures is negated. The answer is that the negated knowledge is different from detailed knowledge and is actually stronger because it knows all parts at once without temporal succession. The passage then addresses two sub-arguments: first, a possible alternative explanation that what is known is the concept of the thing in union with individuals, and second, an analysis of Imam al-Razi's objection that one knowledge (one mental form) cannot correspond to many distinct realities. The author shows that in summary knowledge, the correspondence is between a composite form and a composite, with distributional correspondence of parts, which is unproblematic. The conclusion is that what is negated of God is only knowledge accompanied by ignorance, not the essence of summary knowledge itself.

Key Terms
علم إجمالي (summary knowledge)
الجهل بالتفصيل (ignorance of details)
قاضي (al-Qadi)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
أبو هاشم (Abu Hashim)
وجود ذهني (mental existence)
صورة (form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Summary knowledge is always impossible for God because it implies ignorance.
Correction: Summary knowledge is only impossible if it necessarily includes ignorance of details. If it does not, it is possible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the condition under which summary knowledge is impossible for God, according to the author?
A) That it is different from detailed knowledge
B) That it requires ignorance of details
C) That it is a created knowledge
D) That it involves a composite form

Key Takeaway
Summary knowledge can be affirmed for God as long as it is not conditioned by ignorance of details.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a complex situation with many unknown details, do you trust that God's knowledge encompasses all aspects simultaneously without any ignorance? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to project your own limited way of knowing onto God?

This lesson distinguishes between summary knowledge that is coupled with ignorance and that which is not. In your own life, is your trust in God's knowledge often tainted by the assumption that He must be ignorant of some details? What step will you take this week to rely more fully on His complete knowledge?
?
