The Will is Not Conditioned by Belief in Benefit
Al-Jurjani proves divine will is unconditioned by benefit using examples of equal alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni proof that divine will exists independently of any belief in benefit or inclination, using examples of choice under equal alternatives.

Core Idea
Will can act without any motive of benefit or inclination.

Al-Jurjani presents a clear rational argument to distinguish the divine will from any notion of benefit or inclination, as claimed by the Mu'tazila. By examining common human experiences—like fleeing from a beast or choosing between equal options—he shows that will often operates without any prior belief in benefit. This passage lays out the proof, anticipates objections, and defends the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the opening statement: the will is not conditioned by belief in benefit or consequent inclination. This is the thesis to be proven.
2
Examine the first example: the fleeing person
Focus on the scenario of a person fleeing a beast with two equal paths. Understand why this shows will without a benefit motive. Note the author's clarification that there is a preponderator for the act over non-act, but no motive (dāʿin).
3
Explore the additional examples
Read the examples of the thirsty person with two equal cups and the hungry person with two equal loaves. See how they reinforce the same point.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila objection and the response
The Mu'tazila claim that preference without a preponderator is impossible. Al-Jurjani denies their necessity and counters with his own necessity from the examples. Follow the back-and-forth argument carefully.
5
Reflect on the conclusion and the incomplete ending
The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: will exists without benefit or inclination. Note that the Arabic text is incomplete, but the translation provides the intended closure.
The final sentence about the two traversals being equal and one being preponderated without a motive is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرادة عندنا غير مشروطة باعتقاد النفع أو بميل يتبعه ) وذلك أن الإرادة توجد بدونهما فلا تكون عين أحدهما ولا مشروطة به أيضاً، فلا بأحدهما أصلاً ( خلافاً للمعتزلة ) الذين فسروها بواحد منهما (لنا) في وجود الإرادة بدونهما ( أنَّ الهارب من السبع إذا عن أي ظهر (له طريقان متساويان) في الإفضاء إلى مطلوبه الذي هو النجاة منه ( فإنه ) مع كونه ملجأ في الهرب ( يختار أحدهما ) بإرادته، ولا يتوقف في ذلك الاختيار ) على ترجح أحدهما لنفع) يعتقده ( فيه ولا على ميل يتبعه، بل يرجح أحدهما ) على الآخر ( بمجرد الإرادة لا أقول : لا يكون لفعل مرجح ) على عدمه فإنَّ الهارب بإرادته مرجح إياه على تركه، (بل) أقول : (لا يكون إليه ) أي إلى الفعل ( داع) باعث للفاعل عليه من اعتقاد النفع أو ميل تابع له، ) ومعلوم بالضرورة أنه من دهشته ) وحيرته ( لا يخطر بباله طلب مرجح يختار بسببه أحدهما، بل لا يطلب ولا يتصور في تلك الحالة سوى النجاة (و) معلوم بالضرورة أيضاً ( أنه لو لم يجد المرجح لم يتوقف متفكراً ) فيه ( حتى يفترسه السبع، وكذلك العطشان إذا كان عنده قد حان من ماء وفرض استواؤهما من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما بلا داع له يرجحه في اعتقاده ) على الآخر ) وكذلك جائع عنده رغيفان ) متساويان من جميع الجهات فإنه يختار أحدهما من غير داع يدعوه إليه، وإذا ثبت في هذه الأمثلة وجود الإرادة دون اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه ثبت وجودها بدون الميل التابع لهما، إذ لا وجود للتابع بدون المتبوع ) والمعتزلة ادعوا الضرورة بأن من استوى عنده الطرفان لا يرجح باختياره ( أحدهما ) على الآخر ( إلا لمرجح ) يختص بذلك الطرف، فما دام الاستواء لا يتصور منه ترجيح أصلاً ) والجواب : منع الضرورة والمعارضة بالضرورة الأمثلة المذكورة ) فإنا نعلم بالضرورة وجود الترجيح فيها بلا مرجح وداع كما تحققته، فإن قيل : من البين أن الفعل في هذه الأمثلة راجح على الترك، فلا تساوي فيما بينهما . قلنا : سلوك أحد الطريقين يستلزم ترك سلوك الآخر وبالعكس، فإذا استوى السلوكان فقد استوى سلوك أحدهما وتركه على وجه مخصوص، وهو أن يتركه سالكاً للآخر، وأيضاً السلوكان أمران مقدوران متساويان وقد رجح أحدهما بلا
The will according to us is not conditioned by belief in benefit or by a consequent inclination. That is because will exists without them, so it is neither identical to one of them nor conditioned by them; thus it is not [interpreted] by either of them at all, contrary to the Mu'tazila who interpreted it as one of them. Our proof for the existence of will without them: the one fleeing from a beast when two equal paths appear to him leading to his goal of escape, then while compelled to flee he chooses one of them by his will, and does not pause in that choice upon the preponderance of one over the other due to a benefit he believes in nor upon a consequent inclination. Rather, he prefers one over the other by mere will. I do not say that the act has no preponderator over its non-existence—indeed the fleeing person by his will preponderates it over its abandonment—but I say that there is no motive (dāʿin) impelling the agent towards it from belief in benefit or a consequent inclination. It is known necessarily that from his bewilderment and confusion he does not conceive in his mind the seeking of a preponderator by which to choose one of them; rather he neither seeks nor imagines in that state except escape. And it is also known necessarily that if he did not find a preponderator, he would not delay to reflect until the beast devours him. Similarly, the thirsty person when he has two cups of water, assuming their equality in all respects, chooses one of them without any motive (dāʿin) that preponderates it in his belief over the other. Likewise, the hungry person with two loaves equal in all respects chooses one of them without any motive calling him to it. If it is established in these examples the existence of will without belief in benefit or its presumption, then its existence without the consequent inclination is established, for there is no existence of the consequent without the antecedent. The Mu'tazila claimed necessity that one for whom both sides are equal does not prefer by his choice one over the other except by a preponderator specific to that side; as long as equality remains, no preference can be conceived from him. The answer: denial of the necessity and counter-claim of necessity by the mentioned examples, for we know necessarily the existence of preference in them without a preponderator and without a motive as you have verified. If it is said: it is evident that the act in these examples is preponderant over abandonment, so there is no equality between the two. We say: traversing one of the two paths necessitates abandoning the other, and vice versa. So if the two traversals are equal, then the traversal of one and its abandonment in a specific manner are equal, namely that he abandons it by traversing the other. Also, the two traversals are two equally possible matters, and he has preponderated one of them without...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the divine will (al-irāda) is not conditioned by belief in benefit (iʿtiqād al-nafʿ) or a consequent inclination (mayl yatbaʿuhu). The author begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Sunna: will exists without these, so it is neither identical to nor conditioned by them. Then he presents the proof from the example of a person fleeing a beast who has two equal paths to escape. Despite being compelled to flee, he chooses one path by his will without any benefit-based preference. The author clarifies that this is not to deny a preponderator (murjīḥ) for the act over non-action—since the person does prefer the act—but to deny any motive (dāʿin) from belief in benefit or inclination. The person's state of panic shows he does not even consider a preponderator; he simply chooses. The same reasoning applies to the thirsty person with equal cups and the hungry person with equal loaves. From these examples, the author concludes that will exists without belief in benefit, and consequently without the inclination that depends on it. The Mu'tazila claim it is necessarily impossible to prefer one of two equal alternatives without a preponderator. The author responds by denying that necessity and instead claims our own necessity from the examples, where we directly know preference occurs without such a preponderator. An objection is then raised: in these examples, the act is actually preponderant over abandonment, so there is no real equality. The answer explains that while the act is preponderant over absolute abandonment, when we consider the specific alternatives (e.g., one path vs. the other), the two acts are equal, and the agent chooses one without a benefit-based motive. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Misconception: The will must always be motivated by a belief in benefit or an inclination.
Correction: The passage shows that will can act without any such motive, as in cases of equal alternatives where choice occurs by mere will.

Checkpoint
Which example does the author use to prove that will exists without belief in benefit?

Key Takeaway
Will can exist and operate without any prior belief in benefit or consequent inclination, as demonstrated by everyday examples of choice under equal alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last made a choice between two equally good options, did you feel uneasy without a clear reason? This lesson reveals that will can act without benefit. In your next such decision, will you embrace the freedom of that will, or will you invent a false reason to feel justified?
?
You often credit yourself for making 'wise' choices based on benefit. But how many of your daily decisions are actually driven by mere will, not wisdom? Will you now admit that your will is not the slave of your calculations, and let that humility reshape your self-understanding before God?
?
If God's will is not conditioned by benefit, then His decrees may not align with your perceived good. When you face hardship, will you trust that His will acts without needing to benefit you in your eyes, or will you insist that He must have a reason you can understand?

Infinite Regress Objection and Ash'ari Response
Al-Jurjani explains al-Jubba'i's infinite regress objection and the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris respond to the objection that allowing will to be willed leads to an infinite regress, by distinguishing acquired will from necessary will.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris stop the regress of wills by a necessary will created by God.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a key objection to the specification theory: if will can relate to itself, it would require an infinite chain of wills. Al-Jubba'i raises this as an impossibility. But the Ash'aris have a response: not all wills are acquired; a necessary will from God can end the regress. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the key terms and the structure of objection and response.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's objection
Locate al-Jubba'i's argument: if an empowered will were willed by the agent, it would require another will, leading to an infinite regress.
This is the standard objection against self-referential will.
3
Find the Ash'ari response
Read the response: the regress only follows if all wills are acquired (muktasabah) and willed, but it is not necessary. The regress is cut off by a necessary will (irādah ḍarūriyyah) created by God.
Focus on the term 'irādah ḍarūriyyah' – a key concept.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this defense preserves the Ash'ari view that will can be willed without falling into infinite regress, by positing a foundation of necessary, unacquired will.
This is a classic kalam move: stopping a regress with a necessary first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنفسها لأن إرادتنا ليست مقدورة لنا، وإلا احتاج حصولها فينا إلى إرادة أخرى، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى، اللهم إلا أن يذكروا هذا الفرق على تقدير إقدار الله تعالى إيانا على الإرادة، فإن العلماء بناء على هذا التقدير اختلفوا في أن تلك الإرادة المقدورة هل أوجبه الأشاعرة، إذ لا يصدر فعل من فاعل قادر عالم به ذاكر له إلا بإرادته، وقال الجبائي : يستحيل كون الفاعل للإرادة مريداً لها بإرادة أخرى
قوله: (وقال الجبائي يستحيل إلخ) واحتج بأن الإرادة المقدورة لو كانت مرادة للفاعل لكانت تلك الإرادة الثانية مفتقرة إلى إرادة ثالثة والثالثة إلى رابعة وهلم جراً إلى ما لا نهاية له، ويلزم التسلسل المحال ورد بأن التسلسل إنما يلزم أن لو كانت كل إرادة مكتسبة مرادة مكتسبة، وليس يلزم ذلك، بل أمكن قطع التسلسل بالانتهاء إلى إرادة ضرورية حاصلة للفاعل يخلق الله تعالى كذا في أبكار الأفكار.
...itself, because our will is not within our power for us; otherwise its occurrence in us would require another will, and so on to infinity, unless they mention this difference on the assumption that God Almighty empowers us over will. For the scholars, based on this assumption, differed: Did the Ash'aris require it? Since no action proceeds from an agent who is powerful, knowing, and mindful of it except by his will. And al-Jubba'i said: It is impossible for the agent of will to be a willer of it by another will. His statement: 'And al-Jubba'i said it is impossible etc.' He argued that if the empowered will were willed by the agent, then that second will would depend on a third will, and the third on a fourth, and so on ad infinitum, leading to an impossible regress. The response is that the regress would only be necessary if every acquired will were a willed acquisition, but that is not necessary; rather it is possible to cut off the regress by ending at a necessary will that occurs to the agent, created by God Almighty, as in Abkār al-Afkār.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that under the specifying-attribute definition, will cannot relate to itself because our will is not within our power. If it were, an infinite regress would arise (each will needing another will). However, some scholars allow this on the assumption that God empowers us over our will. The Ash'aris argue that every action of a capable, knowing, and mindful agent requires a will, so if our will is willed, it must be by another will. Al-Jubba'i argues that this leads to an infinite regress (each will needing a prior will). The response is that the regress only occurs if every will is an acquired (muktasabah) and willed (murādah) will, but it is not necessary that all wills are acquired; the regress can be stopped by a necessary will (irādah ḍarūriyyah) that God creates in the agent. This is the Ash'ari position.

إقدار الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position requires an infinite regress of wills because they allow will to be willed.
Correction: The Ash'aris avoid regress by positing a necessary will (irādah ḍarūriyyah) created by God, which is not acquired and thus does not require a prior will.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris avoid the infinite regress when arguing that will can be willed?
A) They deny that will can be willed.
B) They claim the regress is not problematic.
C) They posit a necessary will created by God.
D) They say the regress stops at the first will of the agent.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris escape the infinite regress objection by distinguishing acquired will from necessary will, with the latter created by God as a stopping point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever feel like your will is somehow dependent on something deeper? How does the Ash'ari concept of a 'necessary will' created by God change how you understand your own choices? Will you pause today to acknowledge that even your ability to choose is a gift from God?
?
Al-Jubba'i's argument shows that if every will had to be willed, it would go on forever. Have you ever experienced a kind of 'regress' in your own heart—questioning your intentions, then questioning that intention, and so on? How does the Ash'ari answer free you from that endless loop? What would it look like to rest in the necessary will God has placed in you?
?
The distinction between acquired will and necessary will is subtle. In your daily life, are there areas where you try to 'acquire' your own righteousness or control, rather than relying on God's direct creation of your will? What would change if you surrendered those efforts to God's necessary will?

Will vs. Appetite: A Key Distinction
Distinguishes irada from shahwa with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that will (irada) and appetite (shahwa) are distinct concepts that can occur independently or together, and their logical relationship is 'general and specific in one respect'.

Core Idea
Will and appetite are not the same; rational choice can override desire.

This lesson explores the difference between two key terms: irada (will) and shahwa (appetite). Using everyday examples, the author shows that a person can will something without desiring it, or desire something without willing it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of divine will in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two contrasting examples.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two scenarios: unpleasant medicine and delicious but harmful food. Determine which faculty (will or appetite) is present in each.
3
Understand the relationship
The passage concludes that irada and shahwa have a 'general-special relationship' (عموم من وجه). Reflect on how the examples support this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد شرب دواء كريه غاية الكراهة فيشربه ولا يشتهيه بل يتنفر عنه وقد يشتهي الطعام اللذيذ ولا يريده إذا علم أن فيه هلاكه فقد وجد كل واحدة من الإرادة والشهوة بدون الأخرى وقد يجتمعان في شيء واحد فبينهما عموم من وجه بحسب الوجود، وكذا الحال بين الكراهة والنفرة، إذ في الدواء المذكور وجدت النفرة دون الكراهة المقابلة للإرادة وفي اللذيذ الحرام توجد الكراهة من الزهاد دون النفرة الطبيعية، وقد يجتمعان أيضا في حرام منفور عنه
He intends to drink a very unpleasant medicine, and he drinks it but does not desire it; rather he is averse to it. And he may desire delicious food but not will it if he knows that his destruction is in it. So each of will and appetite has been found without the other, and they may come together in one thing. Thus between them there is a general-special relationship according to existence. The same holds between dislike and aversion, for in the mentioned medicine there is aversion without the dislike that is opposite to will, and in the forbidden delicious food there is dislike from the ascetics without natural aversion, and they may also come together in a forbidden thing that is abhorred.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage makes a distinction between two pairs of terms: irada (will) and shahwa (appetite/desire), and karaha (dislike) and nafra (aversion). It explains by examples: a person may will to drink a very unpleasant medicine out of rational choice (irada) while having no appetite (shahwa) for it, even feeling aversion. Conversely, one may have appetite for delicious food but not will it if harmful. Thus irada and shahwa can exist separately or together, showing a relationship of 'general and specific in one respect'—they overlap in some cases but not others. Similarly, karaha (dislike, as the opposite of irada) and nafra (natural aversion) are distinct: in the medicine, natural aversion exists without rational dislike; in forbidden food, ascetics feel rational dislike without natural aversion. They can also coincide. The passage uses observable human experiences to clarify these conceptual differences.

شهوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will and appetite are identical and always accompany each other.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that will can exist without appetite (medicine example) and appetite without will (food example), so they are distinct.

Checkpoint

A) They are always identical.
B) They are opposites.
C) They have a general-special relationship (عموم من وجه).
D) They never occur together.

Key Takeaway
Will (irada) and appetite (shahwa) are distinct: a person can will something without desiring it, and desire something without willing it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Dislike vs. Aversion: Parallel Distinction
Extends the distinction to karaha and nafra

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that dislike (karaha) and aversion (nafra) are distinct concepts, following the same pattern as will and appetite, with independent occurrences.

Core Idea
Dislike and aversion are as distinct as will and appetite.

Having established the difference between will and appetite, the author now applies the same reasoning to the pair 'dislike' (karaha) and 'aversion' (nafra). Using the same examples, he shows that one can feel aversion without rational dislike, and rational dislike without natural aversion. This parallel strengthens the precision of kalam terminology.

Guided Path

Read the second part of the passage

2

The medicine example shows aversion (nafra) without dislike (karaha). The forbidden food shows dislike without aversion for ascetics.
3
Compare with the first pair
Notice the symmetry: karaha is opposite to irada, and nafra is opposite to shahwa. The same logical relationship applies.

Distinguishing Will from Wishful Desire in Kalam
Defines irada as only for possible concurrent things, unlike tamanni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine will (irada) and how it differs from wishful desire (tamanni) in terms of scope and possibility.

Core Idea
Will only applies to possible concurrent things; wishful desire knows no such bounds.

In this passage, the author sharply distinguishes between two closely related concepts: will and wishful desire. While both involve inclination toward something, they differ fundamentally in what they can attach to. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on divine attributes and avoiding a common confusion that has misled some thinkers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Pay special attention to the key terms: irada, tamanni, maqdur, muqarin, muhal.
The author uses a very concise style; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the core distinction
Notice that irada is restricted to 'possible concurrent' things (maqdur muqarin), while tamanni can relate to intrinsically impossible things and past events.
This difference in scope is the heart of the distinction.
3
Understand the mistake corrected
Some earlier thinkers wrongly considered tamanni a type of irada. See how the verified scholars from both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila unanimously reject that view.
The consensus of both schools strengthens the point.
4
Reflect on the final subtle remark
The passage ends by noting that the 'inclination' (mayl) some call irada is actually more like tamanni. This invites deeper reflection on the precise nature of will.
The author says 'tata'ammal' (so reflect) — this is a call to ponder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنها أي الإرادة غير التمني فإنها لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور مقارن لها عند أهل التحقيق، والتمني قد يتعلق بالمحال الذاتي وبالماضي، وقد توهم جماعة أن التمني نوع من الإرادة، حتى عرفوه بأنه إرادة ما علم أنه لا يقع أو شك وقوعه، واتفق المحققون من الأشاعرة والمعتزلة على أن التمني غير الإرادة، والميل الذي يسمونه إرادة كما مر هو بالتمني أشبه منه بالإرادة فتأمل
That is, will is other than wishful desire, for it only relates to a possible concurrent thing according to the verified scholars. And wishful desire may relate to that which is intrinsically impossible and to the past. And a group supposed that wishful desire is a type of will, such that they defined it as willing what one knows will not occur or doubts its occurrence. And the verified scholars among the Ash‘aris and Mu‘tazila agreed that wishful desire is not will. And the inclination that they call will, as has passed, is more similar to wishful desire than to will. So reflect.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines irada (will) in contrast to tamanni (wishful desire). It states that irada only attaches to something 'possible' (maqdur) and 'concurrent' (muqarin) — meaning the willed act occurs at the time of willing. Tamanni, however, can attach to intrinsically impossible things (like wanting to fly without means) or to past events (like wishing something had happened differently). Some earlier thinkers mistakenly considered tamanni a kind of irada, defining it as willing something known or doubted not to happen. But the verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) from both Ash‘aris and Mu‘tazila agree they are distinct. The passage ends with a subtle point: the 'inclination' (mayl) that some people call irada is actually more like tamanni because it can attach to impossible things, unlike true irada. The reader is urged to reflect (tata'ammal).

مقدور
مقارن
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tamanni (wishful desire) is a type of irada (will).
Correction: Tamanni can attach to impossible and past events, whereas irada only attaches to possible concurrent things, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Any possible thing, past or future.
B) Only impossible things.
C) Only a possible concurrent thing (مقدور مقارن).
D) Only past events.

Key Takeaway
Will is strictly for possible concurrent actions; wishful desire is not will because it can extend to impossible and past events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a regret you hold about the past that you often wish had been different. According to this lesson, that wish is tamanni, not irada. How does that distinction affect how you should direct your will today? Are you willing to leave that tamanni behind and focus your irada on what is possible and concurrent now?
?
When you make a resolution, do you truly intend it as a determined will for a possible concurrent action, or is it a vague wish that you hope will happen? Examine your heart: is your 'irada' really irada, or just tamanni dressed up in will's clothing? What step can you take right now to turn a wish into a genuine will?
?
The author warns that the inclination (mayl) often called will is actually more like tamanni. Where in your life have you mistaken a strong desire for a determined will, only to find it attached to something impossible or past? How can you realign your will with only what is possible and present?

Ash'ari's Thesis: Willing Something is Disliking Its Opposite
Presents Ash'ari's proof that irada and karaha of the opposite are identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's rational argument that willing something is identical to disliking its opposite, and the logical necessity behind this thesis.

Core Idea
Willing and disliking its opposite are not two distinct attributes—they are the same.

Al-Ash'ari and his followers propose a striking thesis: that to will something is precisely to dislike its opposite. This might seem counterintuitive, but it follows from a rigorous logical elimination of all other possibilities. In this lesson, we will unpack the proof and see why this identity is necessary to avoid contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and the proof
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the thesis is stated first, followed by a proof that considers all logical possibilities.
The proof uses a disjunction: either the two are the same, or they are different. If different, they are either similar, opposite, or neither.
2
Identify the three alternatives
The proof examines three possibilities if will and dislike were distinct: they could be similar (mimathil), opposite (did), or something else (mukhalif). Understand each term.
Similar means identical in essence, opposite means contraries that cannot coexist.
3
Understand why the first two are impossible
If they were similar or opposite, they could not coexist in the same subject at the same time, which is necessary for the attributes of the same being.
This is a basic rational principle: two similar attributes would be redundant; two opposites negate each other.
4
Understand the third alternative and its absurdity
If they were neither similar nor opposite, they could coexist. But then each must be able to coexist with the opposite of the other, leading to absurdity—a being willing and unwilling the same thing.
This contradiction forces the conclusion that they must be identical.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The only consistent position is that will and dislike of the opposite are one and the same attribute. Consider how this relates to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ الأشعري وكثير من أصحابه : ( إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده بعينها إذ لو كانت إرادة الشيء ( غيرها) أي غير تلك الكراهة (فإما مثلها أو ضدها فلا تجامعها ) لامتناع اجتماع المتماثلين والمتضادين ( وإما مخالف لها أي أمر لا يماثلها ولا يضادها (فيجامع (ضدها، بل يجامع كل واحدة منهما ضد الأخرى
Shaykh al-Ash‘ari and many of his companions said: Wiling something is precisely the dislike of its opposite. For if the will of something were other than that dislike, then it would be either similar to it or opposite to it, and thus they would not coexist, due to the impossibility of simultaneous opposites or similars; or it would be different from it, i.e., something neither similar nor opposite, so it would coexist with its opposite. Rather, each of them coexists with the opposite of the other.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the thesis of al-Ash‘ari and many of his followers that irada (will) of something is identical to karaha (dislike) of its opposite. The proof is a logical disjunction. First, suppose will and dislike of the opposite were two distinct attributes. Then they are either (a) exactly similar, (b) opposites (contraries), or (c) something else (neither similar nor opposite). If they are similar or opposite, they cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time—excluded by the principles of non-contradiction. If they are something else (neither similar nor opposite), then they could coexist, but then each must be able to coexist with the opposite of the other. This leads to the possibility of willing something while simultaneously willing its opposite (since willing something’s opposite is the same as disliking the original), which is absurd. Therefore, the only consistent conclusion is that will and dislike of the opposite are not two distinct attributes but one and the same. The passage thus forms a complete argument by elimination.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
ضد
متماثل
متضاد
مخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that will and dislike are independent attributes that can vary separately.
Correction: The argument shows that if they were independent, it would lead to contradiction; thus they must be identical.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, if will and dislike of the opposite were distinct and neither similar nor opposite, what would follow?
A) They could never coexist.
B) Each could coexist with the opposite of the other.
C) They would be the same attribute.
D) They would be independent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's logical proof demonstrates that willing something is exactly the same as disliking its opposite, a necessary identity to avoid contradiction in the divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something, do you also dislike its opposite? For example, do you pray for health while secretly disliking sickness as something that could bring you closer to Allah? If your will and dislike are separate, you are internally divided. Ash'ari's argument demands unity. How will you unify your will and dislike in submission to Allah's decree?
?
Consider a recent trial in your life. Did you will the trial to end, and did you dislike its opposite (the trial itself)? Or did you accept that Allah's will for you was perfect, and thus your dislike of the trial was actually your will for its opposite? That is a contradiction. How can you train your heart to will only what Allah wills, and dislike only what Allah dislikes, so that your will is undivided?
?
If Allah's will and dislike are identical, then He does not have internal conflict. Do you have internal conflict in your desires? What is one desire you currently have that contradicts another? How does Ash'ari's argument expose the need for you to seek tawheed in your own soul?

Al-Ash'ari's Proof: Willing a Thing is Aversion to Its Opposite
Al-Ash'ari's argument that willing a thing is identical to aversion to its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that the attributes of will (irada) and aversion (karaha) are identical in essence, differing only in relation to their objects.

Core Idea
Willing and aversion are the same attribute viewed from opposite ends.

Al-Ash'ari presents a sophisticated logical proof that willing a thing and being averse to its opposite are not two separate attributes. He argues that if they were distinct, absurd consequences would follow. This lesson unpacks the proof step by step, revealing the power of kalam reasoning in understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense logical style and the structure of the argument.
The passage may seem complex at first; do not worry about grasping every detail immediately.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Ash'ari states that willing a thing (irada) is the very same as aversion (karaha) to its opposite. Look for this statement at the beginning.
This is a bold claim, especially since irada and karaha seem conceptually distinct.
3
Follow the proof's three-case analysis
The proof examines what would happen if irada and karaha were distinct: they would be either similar (mumathil), opposite (didd), or different (mukhalif). Trace how each case is treated.
The first two are dismissed immediately as impossible in coexistence.
4
Understand the absurd consequence of the third case
If they are different (mukhalif), then each could coexist with the opposite of the other, leading to impossible combinations like willing opposite things simultaneously.
This is the key to the proof: the absurdity forces identity.
5
Grasp the conclusion of identity
Since all other possibilities fail, irada and karaha must be the same attribute, differing only in relation (i'tibar). Reflect on how this resolves the dilemma.
This conclusion has deep implications for understanding God's will and aversion in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد السادس : إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده
قال الشيخ الأشعري وكثير من أصحابه : ( إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده بعينها إذ لو كانت إرادة الشيء غيرها أي غير تلك الكراهة فإما مثلها أو ضدها فلا تجامعها لامتناع اجتماع المتماثلين والمتضادين وإما مخالف لها أي أمر لا يماثلها ولا يضادها فيجامع ضدها، بل يجامع كل واحدة منهما ضد الأخرى، إذ الكراهة صفة ترجح الفعل بالوقوع عين الصفة التي ترجح الترك بأحد طرفي المقدور باللاوقوع، كما أن الإرادة صفة ترجح وقوعه وإن أريد بالشيء المقدور فالمعنى الصفة ترجح أحد طرفي المقدور كالحركة بالوقوع عين الصفة التي ترجح أحد طرفي ذلك المقدور ضده كالسكون باللاوقوع، فالإرادة متعلقة بفعل الشيء عين الكراهة متعلقة من يفعل الضد وكذا الحال في الترك وعلى التقديرين إرادة الشيء قد تتحقق بدون كراهة الضديات بأن لا يخطر الضد بالبال أصلاً، وبالعكس وقد يجتمعان بأن يتصور عنده أيضاً الكراهة، ففي هذه الصورة إرادة الشيء، إما نفس كراهة ضده بالذات إنما التغاير بالاعتبار، فمن حيث التعلق بالشيء يكون إرادة من حيث التعلق بضده، فالشيخ الأشعري ذهب إلى اتحادهما بالذات والدليل ناهض عليه لأنهما كانا متغايرين وإما متماثلان أو متضادين فيمتنع اجتماعهما، أو متخالفان يجوز الانفكاك بينهما وليس لهما ضد واحد وكل متخالفين هذا شأنهما يجوز اجتماع كل منهما مع ضد الآخر، فيلزم اجتماع إرادة الشيء مع إرادة اجتماع كراهته مع إرادته على ما بينه الشارح، وكلاهما محال وعلى ما قررناه اندفع جواب المتن بالمنع والمعارضة، كما لا يخفى على الفطن فهذا ما عندي في تحقيق هذا المقال والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
The Third Type – Sixth Objective: Willing a thing is aversion to its opposite. Al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and many of his companions said: 'Willing a thing is the very same aversion to its opposite, because if willing a thing were other than it, i.e., other than that aversion, then it would be either similar to it or opposite to it, and thus it would not coexist with it due to the impossibility of simultaneous similarity and opposition; or it would be different from it, i.e., something not similar nor opposite, and then it would coexist with its opposite. Rather, each one of them coexists with the opposite of the other.' For aversion is an attribute that preponderates action by occurrence, the very same attribute that preponderates non-occurrence by one of the two sides of the potential, just as will is an attribute that preponderates its occurrence. And if by 'thing' is meant the potential, then the meaning is: the attribute that preponderates one of the two sides of the potential, like motion by occurrence, is the very same attribute that preponderates the opposite of that potential, like rest by non-occurrence. Thus will is related to the act of the thing, the very same aversion is related to the act of the opposite, and similarly regarding non-occurrence. And in both cases, willing a thing may occur without aversion to opposites, that the opposite does not come to mind at all, and vice versa, and they may come together such that aversion is also conceived. In this form, willing a thing is either the very same aversion to its opposite in essence, the difference only in consideration; in relation to the thing it is will, in relation to its opposite it is aversion. Thus al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari held that they are identical in essence, and a proof stands for him: because if they were different, then they would be either similar or opposite, so their coexistence would be impossible; or they would be different (mukhalif), then separation between them is possible and they have no single opposite, and every such pair of different things can each coexist with the opposite of the other, therefore the coexistence of willing a thing with willing its opposite, and the coexistence of aversion to it with willing it, would be entailed – both of which are impossible. And according to what we have established, the rebuttal of the text by denial and objection is repelled, as is not hidden from the intelligent. This is what I have in verifying this discussion, and God knows the truth of the matter.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the Ash'ari doctrine that willing (irada) a thing is identical to being averse (karaha) to its opposite. The commentator first quotes al-Ash'ari's statement and then expands on it. The proof proceeds by considering the possible relations if irada and karaha were distinct: they would be either similar (mumathil), opposite (didd), or different (mukhalif). The first two cases are impossible because similar or opposite attributes cannot coexist in one subject at the same time. The third case, that they are mukhalif (neither similar nor opposite), leads to absurd consequences: if they are mukhalif, each can coexist with the opposite of the other. Specifically, the opposite of irada is irada of the opposite, and the opposite of karaha is karaha of the opposite. Thus, if irada of A and karaha of not-A are different (mukhalif), then irada of A could coexist with karaha of not-A's opposite, which is irada of not-A, and also karaha of not-A could coexist with irada of A's opposite, i.e., karaha of A. This results in impossible combinations like willing opposite things simultaneously or being both averse and willing the same thing. Therefore, irada and karaha must be the same attribute differing only in relation. The commentator also clarifies the definitions of irada and karaha as preponderating forces and explains that they can occur separately or together in the case of contrary considerations.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
كراهة (karaha)
ضد (didd)
متماثل (mumathil)
مخالف (mukhalif)
اتحاد (ittihad)
تغاير (taghayyur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conceptual distinction between irada and karaha implies they are really distinct attributes in God.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues they are identical in essence; the difference is only in consideration (i'tibar) based on the object of relation.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of al-Ash'ari's argument regarding irada and karaha?
A) They are two distinct attributes that can coexist.
B) They are identical in essence, differing only in relation to the object.
C) They are opposites that cannot coexist.
D) They are similar attributes that always occur together.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves that irada and karaha are the same attribute, differing only in relation to the object, thereby resolving an apparent theological dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel both a strong desire to obey Allah and an equally strong aversion to the difficulty of that obedience, can you see them as two faces of the same inner reality? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that your will and your dislike are not separate forces but one attribute directed toward different objects?
?
If Allah's will for your obedience is identical to His aversion to your disobedience, then what does that mean for how you approach moments of temptation? Can you stand before a sin and honestly say that your will to please Allah is the same as your hatred of that sin? If not, what is missing?
?
You have learned that in Allah, irada and karaha are one in essence. How does this transform your understanding of His commands? Do you think He commands you to do what He Himself does not will? Or does His command reveal His very will? If His will is identical to His aversion to its opposite, then what does that say about the urgency of obedience?

Objection and Response on Mukhalif and Didd
Objection to Ash'ari proof and response via talazum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari proof is challenged by denying the coexistence of mukhalif and didd, and how the concept of talazum (mutual entailment) provides the response.

Core Idea
Not all differences allow coexistence; mutual entailment can prevent it.

This lesson examines a key objection to the Ash'ari argument about coexistence of attributes. The objector claims that a thing (mukhalif) can always coexist with the opposite (didd) of another thing. The response introduces talazum (mutual entailment) to show that some pairs cannot be separated. You'll see how logical entailment shapes theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection that a mukhalif can coexist with a didd. Notice the example of motion, blackness, and whiteness.
2
Understand the entailment
The objector then applies this to aversion and willing, leading to a contradiction. Follow the logical chain.
3
Study the response
The commentator rebuts by pointing out talazum: if two things are mutually entailing, one cannot coexist with the opposite of the other.
This blocks the objection.
4
Identify the key terms
Note the terms mukhalif, didd, talazum, malzum, lazim. They are essential for this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (بل يجامع كل واحدة منهما ضد الأخرى هذه الزيادة توطئة لقول المصنف : لكن ضد إرادة الشيء إلخ، فإن ذلك القول إنما يناسب هذه الزيادة لا قوله : فيجامع ضده، بل المخالف للشيء يجوز اجتماعه معه ومع ضده، كالحركة المخالفة للسواد فإنها تجامعه وتجامع البياض أيضاً، ولكن ضد كراهة الضد هو إرادة الضد، فيلزم جواز اجتماع إرادة الشيء مع إرادة ضده، لكن الإرادتين المتعلقتين بالضدين متضادتان فلا يجوز اجتماعهما، وكذا (ضد إرادة الشيء إرادة الضد ) فإذا جوز اجتماع كراهة مع ضد إرادة الشيء ( فيلزم كراهة الضد مع إرادته ) أي يلزم جواز اجتماعهما، وإنما لم يقل : ضد إرادة الشيء كراهة ذلك الشيء فيلزم حينئذ كراهة الضدين، لأن استحالته ممنوعة بخلاف استحالة إرادتهما معا واستحالة إرادة الشيء مع كراهته، ( وأنه ) أي اجتماع كراهة الضد مع إرادته ( محال ، والجواب ) عن استدلال الشيخ أنا ) لا نسلم أن المخالف للشيء يجامع ضده لجواز تلازمهما ) أي تلازم الشيء وتخالفه بأن يكون كل منهما ملزوما للآخر، ولا شك أن الملزوم يمتنع ضد اجتماعه مع اللازم، فلا يجوز حينئذ اجتماع شيء من المتخالفين ضد صاحبه (و) جواز قوله : ( ولكن ضد كراهة الشيء الضد هو إرادة الضد، وإن جعل ضمير كانت للكراهة، لأن الضمير يرد إلى أقرب المذكورات، وضمير غيرها للإرادة وكذا الضمير المستتر في فيجامع ضدها للكراهة، والبارز للإرادة لم يحتج إلى تلك المقدمات ويكون المحال اللازم حينئذ اجتماع كراهة الضد مع إرادته فقط هو المذكور في المتن، ويكون الكلام هو المذكور في المتن، ويكون الكلام منتظماً حق الانتظام ويكون موافقاً لما هو مختار المصنف من جواز إرادة الضدين كما سيجيء. قوله: (متضادتان) ليس المراد المعنى المصطلح لعدم كونها لذاتهما، بل المعنى لا يجتمعان في محل لاستلزامهما ترجيح الضدين الضدين معاً. قوله : ( أي يلزم إلخ) قدر الجواز لانه اللازم من قوله : إذا المخالف للشيء يجوز الاجتماع ضده. ، ومع المناسب له أن يقال : لكن ضده كراهة الضد إلخ، كما أشار إليه الشارح وهو ظاهر جداً ثم هذه الزيادة ليست زيادة أمر لا يفهم من كلام المصنف، بل يفهم من قوله : إذا المخالف للشيء يجوز اجتماعه معه ضده، كما لا ومع كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( ولكن ضد كراهة الضد إلخ إنما لم يقل : ضد كراهة الضد كراهة ضد هذا الضد كما أن ضد كراهة القعود كراهة القيام الذي هو ضده، لأن استحالة كراهة الضدين ممنوع سيذكره الآن وليس كراهة الضدين ضدين . قوله : ( بخلاف استحالة إرادتهما قد يمنع المصنف تلك الاستحالة أيضاً بعيد هذا لكن ) غرض الشارح هاهنا تقرير دليل الشيخ على وفق مدعاه وفيه إيماء إلى اندفاع اعتراض المقاصد على استلزام إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده، بتقدير المغايرة على أن منع المصنف يدفعه تفسير الأشاعرة للإرادة كما ستطلع عليه . قوله: (لجواز تلازمهما فإن قلت : المتخالفان قسم من المتغايرين، والملازمة لا تجتمع
His saying: 'Rather, each one of them coexists with the opposite of the other' – this addition is a preparation for the author's saying: 'But the opposite of willing a thing is such and such...' For that saying suits this addition, not his saying: 'so it coexists with its opposite.' Rather, a thing that is different (mukhalif) can coexist with that thing and with its opposite, like motion which is different from blackness, for it coexists with it and also with whiteness. But the opposite of aversion to the opposite is willing the opposite, so the permissibility of coexistence of willing a thing with willing its opposite is entailed, yet two wills related to opposites are themselves opposite, so their coexistence is not allowed. Similarly, (the opposite of willing a thing is willing the opposite) – so if one permits coexistence of aversion with the opposite of willing a thing, then it entails aversion to the opposite with willing it, i.e., the permissibility of their coexistence is entailed. And he did not say: the opposite of willing a thing is aversion to that same thing, because then it would entail aversion to both opposites, but its impossibility is not conceded, unlike the impossibility of willing both together and the impossibility of willing a thing with aversion to it. And that, i.e., the coexistence of aversion to the opposite with willing it, is impossible. And the answer to al-Shaykh's proof is: we do not concede that a mukhalif thing can coexist with its opposite, due to the possibility of their mutual entailment (talazum), i.e., the thing and its mukhalif being such that each is a necessary condition for the other. Without doubt, the necessary condition (malzum) cannot have its opposite coexist with its necessary consequent (lazim). Therefore, no two mukhalif things can coexist with the opposite of the other...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the Ash'ari proof and the commentator's response. The objector challenges the premise that a mukhalif (different-in-kind thing) can coexist with a didd (opposite). He gives the example of motion and blackness: motion is different from blackness and can coexist with both blackness and its opposite whiteness. However, the opposite of aversion to a given opposite is willing that opposite, so if a mukhalif can coexist with the opposite, it would lead to the coexistence of willing a thing and willing its opposite, which is impossible. The objector thus denies the initial claim about mukhalif and didd. The answer is that the denial is based on the possibility of talazum (mutual entailment) between the two. If the thing and its mukhalif are such that one entails the other, then they cannot be separated. In that case, the mukhalif cannot coexist with the opposite of the other because that would require the coexistence of the malzum and the opposite of its lazim, which is impossible. The commentator also discusses alternative grammatical readings and the author's view that willing two opposites is possible, but that is not the focus here.

تلازم
ملزوم
لازم
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about empirical incompatibility.
Correction: The objection is about logical entailment; the response uses talazum to block coexistence only when mutual entailment exists.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator rebut the objection that a mukhalif can always coexist with a didd?
A) By stating that mukhalif and didd are always opposite and cannot coexist.
B) By showing that talazum (mutual entailment) prevents the claimed coexistence.
C) By denying that any two attributes can be mukhalif.
D) By appealing to the impossibility of willing two opposites.

Key Takeaway
The key to the response is talazum: when two things are mutually entailing, they cannot be separated, so the mukhalif cannot coexist with the didd of the other.

Does Will Add a Positive Attribute?
The author presents and refutes the claim that will adds no attribute, distinguishing relative from positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that will does not add a positive attribute to its object, and why the author holds that it only adds a relative description.

Core Idea
Will adds only a relative description, not a real attribute, to its object.

This lesson examines a key dispute in kalām about whether the divine will (or any will) adds a real, existing attribute to the action or statement that is willed. Al-Qadi and al-Basri argue that it does not, while the author refines their position by distinguishing between positive and relative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the passage begins with a condition on the definition of will, then presents the claim of al-Qadi and al-Basri, and finally the author's response.
Pay attention to the terms 'thubūtī' (positive) and 'iʿtibārī' (relative).
2
Understand the conditional opening
The first sentence says the previous statement (that will does not add an attribute) only holds if will is defined as 'belief in benefit'. Under the Ash'ari definition, it fails because willing opposites would be impossible.
3
Examine the claim of al-Qadi and al-Basri
They say will gives its object a description like obedience/disobedience or command/threat, not an additional attribute.
4
See the author's response
The author distinguishes: if they mean a positive attribute existing externally, this is denied; if they mean a relative description, it is uncontroversial and trivial.
The author uses the example of statements to show that a positive attribute cannot subsist in something that has no simultaneous existence.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that will only adds a relative description, not a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة
الذي ذكره إنما يتأتى إذا فسرت الإرادة باعتقاد النفع أو ما يتبعه، وأما إذا فسرت بصفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي الفعل مقارنة كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فلا لأن إرادة الضدين تستلزم اجتماعهما معاً.
( قال القاضي من الأشاعرة وأبو عبد الله البصري من المعتزلة : لا إرادة تفيد متعلقها صفة زائدة على ذات المتعلق سواء كان المتعلق فعلاً أو قولاً، فللفعل تفيد كونه طاعة كالسجود بإرادته لله تعالى، ومعصية كالسجود بإرادته للصنم، وللقول تفيد كونه أمراً أو تهديداً. فإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة ثبوتية موجودة في الخارج منع كون الإرادة كذلك، وما ذكراه من كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية وكون القول أمراً أو تهديداً وصف اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، كيف والقول لا وجود لجملته معاً، فكيف تقوم به صفة وجودية. وإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة اعتبارية، فذلك مما لا ينازع فيه ولا يتصور في ذكره مزيد فائدة.
قوله : ( كما هو رأي الأشاعرة ) فإن القصد المتقدم على الفعل يلزم عليه وليس بإرادة كما مر، فظهر ضعف ما في شرح المقاصد من القول بأن متعلق الإرادة الحادثة لا يكون إلا مقارناً للإرادة حتى ما يكون متعلقاً بالمستقبل يكون من قبيل النهي مخالف للغة والعرف والتحقيق.
قوله : ( ومنع كون الإرادة كذلك ) كيف ولو كانت الإرادة موجبة لصفة وجودية ومؤثرة لها لانقلبت الإرادة قدرة، لثبوت أخص صفة القدرة كذا في أبكار الأفكار.
The third type - the seventh topic: Will and its relation to the attribute.
What he mentioned is only plausible if will is interpreted as belief in benefit or what follows it. But if it is interpreted as an attribute that particularizes one of the two sides of the action, concurrent (with the action) as is the view of the Ash'aris, then it is not, because willing two opposites entails their simultaneous existence.
Al-Qadi (of the Ash'aris) and Abu Abdullah al-Basri (of the Mu'tazila) said: No will gives its object an attribute additional to the essence of the object, whether the object is an action or a statement. For the action, it gives it being obedience—like prostrating by willing it for Allah—or disobedience—like prostrating by willing it for an idol. For the statement, it gives it being a command or a threat. If they meant that it gives its object a positive attribute existing externally, then [the claim that] will is like that is denied. What they mentioned—that the action is obedience or disobedience and the statement is a command or threat—is a relative description (waṣf iʿtibārī) with no actual existence externally. How? The statement as a whole has no simultaneous existence, so how can a positive attribute subsist in it? And if they meant that it gives its object a relative description, then that is undisputed and no further benefit is conceivable in mentioning it.
His statement: (as is the view of the Ash'aris) — for the intention (al-qasd) prior to the action is entailed by it and is not will, as has passed. Thus the weakness of what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid—that the object of temporally originated will (al-irāda al-ḥāditha) must be concurrent with the will, even what is related to the future is of the category of prohibition (al-nahy)—appears, for it contradicts language, custom, and verification.
His statement: (and [they] denied that will is such) — how? For if will necessitated a positive attribute and was effective in it, then will would turn into power, because the most specific attribute of power is established. This is mentioned in Abkār al-Afkār.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative discussion about the nature of will (al-irāda) and whether it adds an attribute to its object. It begins by referencing a previous statement that the author considers conditional on a particular interpretation of will—if will is defined as belief in benefit, then the claim may hold, but if defined as an Ash'ari specifying attribute concurrent with the action, then willing opposites would be impossible. Then it reports the claim of al-Qadi and al-Basri that will does not give its object any additional attribute; rather, it only makes the action obedient or disobedient, or the statement a command or threat. The author responds: if they meant a positive (thubūtiyya) attribute existing externally, this is denied because the descriptions they mention (obedience, disobedience, command, threat) are merely relative (iʿtibārī) and have no external existence—especially for statements, which have no simultaneous existence. If they meant a relative description, then it is uncontroversial and mentioning it is pointless. The commentary then explains that the reference to the Ash'ari view points out that 'intention' (al-qasd) prior to action is not will, contradicting a claim in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that temporally originated will must have a concurrent object. Finally, the passage argues that if will produced a positive attribute, it would become power, because effecting is the most specific property of power.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الصفة
اعتباري
ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will adds a positive attribute to its object.
Correction: Will only adds a relative description (iʿtibārī), not a real existing attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do al-Qadi and al-Basri claim about the effect of will on its object?
A) It adds a positive attribute existing externally.
B) It only gives the object a relative description like obedience or command.
C) It creates a new essence in the object.
D) It has no effect at all.

Key Takeaway
Will does not confer a real attribute; it only gives the action or statement a relative description like obedience or command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship like prayer or charity, do you view it merely as an action that gains a relative description of 'obedience' because of your intention, or do you feel it adds a real, inherent quality to the act itself? How might this distinction affect your sincerity and your reliance on Allah's acceptance rather than the act's inherent goodness?
?
The passage says that calling a statement a 'command' or 'threat' is merely a relative description with no external existence. When you speak words that carry authority or warning, do you treat them as weighty realities that affect your accountability to Allah, or do you consider them just relative labels? How does this lesson challenge you to take your words more seriously?
?
If will only gives a relative description, then the same action (e.g., prostrating) can be obedience or disobedience based on intention. How does this make you examine the intentions behind your daily actions? Are you satisfied that your actions are merely labelled 'good' by relative description, or do you yearn for an intrinsic worth that only Allah can grant?

Further Arguments: Will, Intention, and Power
Additional arguments against will adding a positive attribute: the concurrency issue and the power transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional arguments about the temporality of will, the distinction between will and intention, and why will cannot produce a positive attribute on pain of becoming power.

Core Idea
If will produced a real attribute, it would become power; and will is not prior intention.

This lesson continues the discussion on will by addressing two further points: first, the Ash'ari view that will is concurrent with its object contradicts the distinction between will and prior intention; second, if will were to effect a real attribute, it would be indistinguishable from power. These arguments reinforce that will's effect is merely relative.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about Ash'ari will being concurrent
The author criticizes the view that temporally originated will must have a concurrent object. He argues that this contradicts language, custom, and verification, and confuses will with prior intention.
Reference is made to Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid for the opposing view.
2
Grasp the argument that will cannot produce a positive attribute
If will necessitated and effected a positive attribute, then will would become power, because effecting is the most specific attribute of power.
This is presented as a logical consequence, citing Abkār al-Afkār.
3
Connect to the earlier distinction
These arguments support the conclusion that will's effect is only a relative description, not a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد السابع : الإرادة وتعلقها بالصفة
الذي ذكره إنما يتأتى إذا فسرت الإرادة باعتقاد النفع أو ما يتبعه، وأما إذا فسرت بصفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي الفعل مقارنة كما هو رأي الأشاعرة فلا لأن إرادة الضدين تستلزم اجتماعهما معاً.
( قال القاضي من الأشاعرة وأبو عبد الله البصري من المعتزلة : لا إرادة تفيد متعلقها صفة زائدة على ذات المتعلق سواء كان المتعلق فعلاً أو قولاً، فللفعل تفيد كونه طاعة كالسجود بإرادته لله تعالى، ومعصية كالسجود بإرادته للصنم، وللقول تفيد كونه أمراً أو تهديداً. فإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة ثبوتية موجودة في الخارج منع كون الإرادة كذلك، وما ذكراه من كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية وكون القول أمراً أو تهديداً وصف اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، كيف والقول لا وجود لجملته معاً، فكيف تقوم به صفة وجودية. وإن أرادا أنها تفيد متعلقها صفة اعتبارية، فذلك مما لا ينازع فيه ولا يتصور في ذكره مزيد فائدة.
قوله : ( كما هو رأي الأشاعرة ) فإن القصد المتقدم على الفعل يلزم عليه وليس بإرادة كما مر، فظهر ضعف ما في شرح المقاصد من القول بأن متعلق الإرادة الحادثة لا يكون إلا مقارناً للإرادة حتى ما يكون متعلقاً بالمستقبل يكون من قبيل النهي مخالف للغة والعرف والتحقيق.
قوله : ( ومنع كون الإرادة كذلك ) كيف ولو كانت الإرادة موجبة لصفة وجودية ومؤثرة لها لانقلبت الإرادة قدرة، لثبوت أخص صفة القدرة كذا في أبكار الأفكار.
The third type - the seventh topic: Will and its relation to the attribute.
What he mentioned is only plausible if will is interpreted as belief in benefit or what follows it. But if it is interpreted as an attribute that particularizes one of the two sides of the action, concurrent (with the action) as is the view of the Ash'aris, then it is not, because willing two opposites entails their simultaneous existence.
Al-Qadi (of the Ash'aris) and Abu Abdullah al-Basri (of the Mu'tazila) said: No will gives its object an attribute additional to the essence of the object, whether the object is an action or a statement. For the action, it gives it being obedience—like prostrating by willing it for Allah—or disobedience—like prostrating by willing it for an idol. For the statement, it gives it being a command or a threat. If they meant that it gives its object a positive attribute existing externally, then [the claim that] will is like that is denied. What they mentioned—that the action is obedience or disobedience and the statement is a command or threat—is a relative description (waṣf iʿtibārī) with no actual existence externally. How? The statement as a whole has no simultaneous existence, so how can a positive attribute subsist in it? And if they meant that it gives its object a relative description, then that is undisputed and no further benefit is conceivable in mentioning it.
His statement: (as is the view of the Ash'aris) — for the intention (al-qasd) prior to the action is entailed by it and is not will, as has passed. Thus the weakness of what is in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid—that the object of temporally originated will (al-irāda al-ḥāditha) must be concurrent with the will, even what is related to the future is of the category of prohibition (al-nahy)—appears, for it contradicts language, custom, and verification.
His statement: (and [they] denied that will is such) — how? For if will necessitated a positive attribute and was effective in it, then will would turn into power, because the most specific attribute of power is established. This is mentioned in Abkār al-Afkār.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative discussion about the nature of will (al-irāda) and whether it adds an attribute to its object. It begins by referencing a previous statement that the author considers conditional on a particular interpretation of will—if will is defined as belief in benefit, then the claim may hold, but if defined as an Ash'ari specifying attribute concurrent with the action, then willing opposites would be impossible. Then it reports the claim of al-Qadi and al-Basri that will does not give its object any additional attribute; rather, it only makes the action obedient or disobedient, or the statement a command or threat. The author responds: if they meant a positive (thubūtiyya) attribute existing externally, this is denied because the descriptions they mention (obedience, disobedience, command, threat) are merely relative (iʿtibārī) and have no external existence—especially for statements, which have no simultaneous existence. If they meant a relative description, then it is uncontroversial and mentioning it is pointless. The commentary then explains that the reference to the Ash'ari view points out that 'intention' (al-qasd) prior to action is not will, contradicting a claim in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that temporally originated will must have a concurrent object. Finally, the passage argues that if will produced a positive attribute, it would become power, because effecting is the most specific property of power.

Correction: Will is concurrent with its object, while intention precedes the action and is not will.

Objection and Response on Created Ability
Objection and response on the definition of created ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response to the objection that created ability (qudra haditha) does not fit the definitions of ability because it does not produce actual effects.

Core Idea
Created ability is inherently suited to effect but preempted by divine power.

We now encounter a critical objection to the Ash'arite definition of ability. The objector argues that created ability fails to meet either definition because it does not produce any actual effect. The response from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies that ability is defined by its inherent suitability to effect, not by actual effect. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Notice how it claims created ability fits neither definition.
The objection uses the Ash'arite concepts of first and second interpretation.
2
Understand the response
Read the response from Sharh al-Maqasid. Observe how it reinterprets 'sha'n al-ta'thir' as inherent suitability rather than actual effect.
The response also mentions support from the temporality of objects of power.
3
Grasp the final nuance
The passage ends with an important point about semantic commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi). Reflect on how this resolves the issue.
This connects to broader kalam debates on attribute universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد عليهما أي على التفسيرين القدرة الحادثة على رأينا معاشر الأشاعرة. فإنها لا تؤثر في فعل أصلاً، فلا تدخل في التفسير الأول، وليست مبدأ الأثر قطعاً فلا تدخل في التفسير الثاني، وإن كان لها عندنا تعلق بالفعل يسمى ذلك التعلق كسباً. قوله: ( ويرد عليهما إلخ) أجاب عنه في شرح المقاصد بأن المراد من شأنها التأثير، ولا أن القدرة الحادثة كذلك، لكن لوقوع المقدور بالقدرة لم يؤثر بالفعل ويؤيد ذلك قولنا بحدوث متعلقات القدرة القديمة، وقول المعتزلة بتقدم القدرة الحادثة على الفعل بالزمان هذا، لكن إثبات القدرة الحادثة من شأنها التأثير دونه خرط القتاد كيف وقد قام البرهان على امتناع تأثيرها، والقول بأن القدرة القائمة مؤثرة، فيكون من شأن الحادثة التأثير أيضاً لاشتراكهما في مطلق القدرة، إنما يتم على القول بالاشتراك المعنوي، وبأن تأثير القدرة ليس بخصوصية ذاتها، بل لكونها قدرة.
And it is objected to them, i.e., to the two interpretations, [that] the created ability according to our view, the Ash'arites. For it does not effect any act at all, so it does not enter into the first interpretation, and it is not a source of effect definitively, so it does not enter into the second interpretation, and [yet] it has according to us a connection to the act called acquisition (kasb). He said: (And it is objected to them etc.) He answered it in Sharh al-Maqasid that the intended [meaning] is that it is in its nature to effect, not that the created ability is like that, but because the object of power occurs by [divine] power, it does not effect actually. And this is supported by our statement of the temporality of the objects of eternal power, and the Mu'tazilite statement of the precedence of the created ability over the act in time. However, establishing that the created ability is such as to effect is extremely difficult, especially since proof has been established of the impossibility of its effect. And the statement that the [divine] power is effective, so the created one should also be effective due to their sharing in the absolute concept of ability, is only valid on the view of semantic commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi), and that the effect of ability is not by the specificity of its essence but by its being ability.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the Ash'arite definition of ability (qudra) and its response. The objection states that the created ability (qudra haditha) does not produce any actual effect, so it fails to meet both interpretations of the definition: neither as a source of effect nor as a principle of effect. The answer from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies that ability is defined by its inherent suitability to effect (sha'n al-ta'thir), not by actual effect. The created ability possesses this suitability, but its effect is overridden by divine power, as the object of power occurs by God's power. The response also notes the difficulty of proving that created ability is inherently suitable given that proof has established its impossibility. The final point affirms that divine power is effective due to its essence as power, not due to any specific attribute, and this applies to created ability only under semantic commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi), meaning both share the same concept of ability.

Key Terms
قدرة
كسب
تأثير
شأن التأثير
اشتراك معنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is an ability, it must always produce its effect.
Correction: Ability is defined by its inherent suitability to effect, not by actual effect; created ability has that suitability but its effect is preempted by divine power.

Checkpoint
According to the response in Sharh al-Maqasid, how should the created ability (qudra haditha) be understood?
A) It is not a source of effect at all.
B) It is inherently suited to effect but does not actually effect because the object occurs by divine power.
C) It effects independently of divine power.
D) It is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
Created ability is inherently suited to effect (sha'n al-ta'thir) but does not actually effect because the object of power occurs by divine power.

The objector assumed that if your ability doesn't produce an effect, it is worthless. The Ash'arite response affirms its value as a 'suitability' and a connection called acquisition. Do you treat your own capacities as if they have no role unless they produce visible results? Consider a moment when you felt your ability was insufficient or failing. The lesson says proof shows the impossibility of your ability effecting anything on its own. Does that realization make you feel helpless or liberated?
